	Some comments on Jim Selders' thought-provoking contribution:

>>  I've never heard the name Deming in relation to Porsche.  
>>  Unfortunately their way of doing statistical analysis is to build
>>  10,000 cars with a bug, get lots of warranty claims, and then 
>>  redesign the part.

	Uh-oh.  This is exactly my concern.

>>  But those Germans can be proud and stubborn, so who knows what they're up to

*If* they (a fine German company such as Porsche) would implement 
a program of quality improvement, I'm sure that they would do a 
fine job of it.  To be successful, ongoing quality improvement 
requires dedication and commitment - surely strengths of Porsche.
My concern is that the transition takes courage to depart radically
from the way things have been done, and this is *not* an easy thing to do,
especially for a tradition-rich company such as Porsche.

	The irony of these situations - when a fundamental change occurs
in a business (in this case, auto manufacturing according to new design 
principles) - is that the successful companies are typically the *most* 
resistant to change ... and therefore the most likely to suffer.  
Having worked for a German firm (Siemens, in Munich) for several years, 
I agree with Jim's comment that German firms can be *very* resistant 
to change.


>>  In any event, applying Deming techniques would improve the quality and 
>>  reduce the warranty cost, both direct and indirect (lost customers).  
>>  The net effect is more money in Porsche's pocket and the consumer's.  
>>  Sounds like a win-win!  

	Exactly!


>>  Hey, let's get somebody from Weissach on this mailing list!

	Good idea.  I wish that we could use this forum to provide
feedback and suggestion to lots of services that see us as "customers:" 

	* SCCA (being attempted in autocross mailer)
	* Pacific regional autocross director (being done in autocross mailer)
	* ESPN & ABC motorsports (done on an ad hoc basis, in autocross, when 
	  some comments are gathered and mailed to their programming director)


>>  On the subject of product pricing and positioning, some simple economics can
>>  illustrate Porsche's situation.  
>>  Porsche needs a certain level of income/cash flow to stay in business, 
>>  and more to develop new products.  They are a very small company with 
>>  low sales figures.  Some of you already know about product production 
>>  and sales, but let me give two short examples to illustrate my point.
>>
>>  <some stuff deleted> ...
>>  But if they make a Carrera 2/4 at the high end of the market 
>>  for $40,000 and sell it for $60,000, they now have $20,000 profit per unit
>>  With the volume of that product line (10K-25K), they stand to make 
>>  perhaps $200-$500 million profit.  This is the fundamental reason 
>>  for wanting to move up-market: the margins are huge!  Porsche needs 
>>  these margins to stay in business, since they aren't diversified enough 
>>  to have income from other areas help their bottom line.

	My fear is that they are moving upmarket \\not because they want to
sell more extravagant cars// (though there has been decision to decrease
production below the level of the" 944 boom" years, to prevent oversupply
of the car from reducing demand), but \\because they have to//, in response to 
increased competitive pressure (similar price/performance offered by 
Japanese products).  If this is the case, then running from the competition 
to a new arena will *not* solve their problems.

	I think that the move upscale is forced by the performance/price 
that Japanese cars provide.  I am disturbed by the arguments that are 
made in defense of Porsche which revolve around the financial *need* to 
make more costly cars.  IFF a company produces a superior product, then 
they can be financially successful making any kind of car that they want.  
Porsche makes exclusive sports cars, but Japan offers similar performance
at much lower costs now.  Knowing that price/performance is not linear,
do we really expect Porsche to be able to fundamentally enhance their 
product (relative to the Japanese) by spending incrementallly more 
on their cars?  And what prevents the Japanese firms from following suit,
now that they can compete with Porsche?

	The reality is that Porsche probably has higher development costs 
than the Japanese, so they have a choice:

	* stand and fight (requires cultural change, to become more efficient
	  producer, in order to compete)

	* move upscale (risky, requires new products that provide 
	  incremental value) 

I hope that Weissach's new product strategy (in providing incremental value)
is more than simply "exclusivity" because that depends on people paying more 
for less performance.  I think that Porsche is moving out of the segment 
because it has become too congested with new competitors, and their 
heritage and desire for "exclusivity" gives them an excuse to move upscale.

	It *may* be that in order to make the cars that Porsche wants to 
make, that they will become more costly to produce.  But I think that this 
move is being forced by their competition, and it worries me.


>>  I love Porsches, but have to admit that the 300ZX Turbo is a 
>>  very competitive car for lots less money.  
>>  The point is that Porsche is over-priced for most consumers
>>  - the dollar/value relationship isn't perceived as very good.  

>>  Porsche can't be competitive with Japan Inc. due to the higher material 
>>  quality and cost of business/production in Germany.  And I don't think 
>>  they want to be, which is why they're moving up-scale with their products.  >>  Porsche, Ferrari, etc. didn't get their fine names by building a car that 
>>  lots of people could afford.  

	I guess that Kevin has hit here at root of the problem: 

   I want to continue to be able to afford Porsches, 
   but Porsche is starting to look more like Ferrari (unreachable)
   with this move.  

I like Porsche, and I hate to see them leave me and many other customers 
behind (and leaving us with one fewer alternative to the Japanese products).


	Competition is healthy, Porsche; stay and fight.  Get lean and mean.

		Robert

Here are a few questions which have accumulated recently.

1. Mobil was perhaps the first fuel brand which earned a good rep in injected
cars because of its detergent content.  Yet one cannot help noticing Mobil's
consistent absence these days from manufacturers' lists of approved fuels.  Why?

2. Has anyone chipped their '87 or '88 944S with the Autothority chip set?  With
what results?  How has the low-end driveability been affected?  Anyone ponied up
for a chip set from Porsche Motorsports?

3. Has anyone grafted a 944S2 air induction system onto an '87 or '88 944S?  The
theory is that it yields a greater air flow rate and a bit of natural boost at
the manifold.  This allows the fuel flow to be increased, with correspondingly
better performance.

4. Can anyone recommend tires for a 928 which sees all-year driving, but little
snow if its owner can avoid it?  Judging from the recent 951 tire thread, the
following might be appealing:

  Bridgestone RE71 VR4
  Goodrich Comp T/A, V-rated not H-rated
  Dunlop D40-M2
  Pirelli P700 (except nobody has really used 'em)

Kevin

One point should be mentioned regarding Porsche's production gaffs.  Sure, the
951 had problems with its exhaust header (port liners) when it came out.  But
fixing them didn't cost their owners a red cent.  And someone may indeed have
gone through several manifolds before they got it right, but Porsche stood right
behind them.

PCNA has been extremely generous with me and everone else I know in fixing
busted stuff, even out of warranty.  No squawks from the dealer or rep about,
oh, that funny looking sway bar, or the different ducting, or, say, those brake
lines aren't original, are they?  Were fire extinguishers standard equipment in
'87?  Hmm, 245/45 A008R's to drive to the shopping mall, Mr. Gross?  Danged if I
can imagine any other manufacturer who would stand behind the car this way.

I think that we have to cut Porsche a bit of slack in the quality department. 
They are producing, as Jim pointed out, almost race-ready cars.  When you push
the envelope, things might break.  You go out and hammer your 951 at the track
and autocross, and maybe the clutch friction material works loose.  So you get
it fixed, Porsche sees this happening a lot, and rev's the part.  What else can
you expect?

Porsche doesn't sell 100,000 units of anything a year, and they don't take ten
years to produce a wuss copy of a 'real' car like the Japanese do.

KG

Subject: Re: Porsche market position 
	
	sell more extravagant cars// (though there has been decision to decrease
	production below the level of the" 944 boom" years, to prevent oversupply
	of the car from reducing demand), but \\because they have to//, in response to 
	increased competitive pressure (similar price/performance offered by 

I believe that the real reason to decrease overall sales volumes relates
the need to commit significant investment up front in manufacturing
capacity when the ability to sell that volume depends on a variable
that Porsche cannot control ($/mark). Porsche got in over their head in the
early-mid 80's by significantly increasing their 944 sales volumes in the
US through attractive pricing, commiting (to Audi) for significant
increased manufacturing volume, and then having the $/mark ratio change
to make their cars less competitive, making the additional manufacturing
capacity unneeded. I believe that the statements that Porsche made at
the time said that they would never again commit that degree of their
business future to a foreign market. This essentially forces Porsche
up-market, since the largest opportunity for high volume sales of
their cars is the US.

This does not in any way imply that Porsche should not focus on quality
improvement, but it does say there are other business reasons to move
to high-cost/low-volume if you are a relatively small company. I, by
the way, also deplore the implications to us middle class enthusiasts,
but I guess we will have to settle for used Porsches.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

early 86) 944T that I should know about?  Any particular problems

I did a similar operation on a '73.  I noticed, actually, that the
cylinder is remarkably identical to those from VWs of that era.  Although
I could have rummaged through my spare parts boxes and disasseble a
handful of locks to find the right pins, I used the brute-force method
instead.  As follows:

	1. Remove cylinder from lock.

	2. Insert key which you would like to operate lock.  This
	   engages the pins; if you inserted the proper key, all pins
	   would be retracted.

	3. File down any protruding pins.

	4. Remove key, reverse, and repeat step 3 for the other
	   orientation.

	5. Reinstall cylinder.

After this procedure, you'll have a re-keyed lock.  The original key
will still work, as will probably a million other keys, but its a
quick-and-dirty method to get your key to fit.

If you have some security issues with this procedure, try the following
(which I'd recommend instead).  Remove the glove compartement lock.  If
the previous owners of your car are anything like the rest of the world,
that lock is pristine.  Take the lock to a locksmith and have him make a
key from it -- you'll get an ORIGINAL key back, or pretty damn close to
one.  Now, swap cylinders between the door lock (non-re-keyed) and the
glove-compartement lock.  Voila: you have a NEW lock on your door, and a
secure glove compartement, much more convenient than two different door
locks.

Cheers!

	- pz.

one: make sure the original chain has been replaced. It's supposed to be
done every 30K miles. Otherwise you're apt to be in for Big Bucks one of
these days. I got that advice from everyone I talked to when I was shopping
for a 944. (And wound up with a 951 which I love.)


Patty

Uh, I would look further than the 'early' car you've found.  If it's smacked a
wall, the frame is in all likelihood our of kilter.  It can be checked and if
necessary straightened, but you'll pay dearly for it.

The oil leaks can be fixed too.  Very often 944's develop leaking balance shaft
seals.  They can be easily replaced, if I am not mistaken, but not before you've
pulled off the timing belt and a bunch of other stuff.  More money.  However,
your timing belt may need replacement anyways.

I have never heard of Tokiko shocks on a 944.  Most folks I know stick with Koni
or Bilstein.  I have the sport Konis, and they work fine (except when the rears
leak!).

There are a fair number of options available for the car.  Pick up a PCNA '86
Fact Book through a dealer or mail order; it lists all the options and their
codes and prices.  To find out what your prospective Garage Queen has, unpop the
snaps holding down the rear carpet over the spare tire.  On the body wall you
will find a sticker listing the car's build list, ie, all its options.

The early 951's had problems with their exhaust manifolds and port liners, I
believe.  Porsche replaced them for free on demand, but not everyone noticed the
leak and asked.  They have also replaced the fuel hose(s?) on the 951.  If your
has a crimp in the end where it meets the rail (I think), it's an original part
and MUST be replaced.  There is currently a recall campaign for all 944-series
cars: some get the fuel hose, some get the rail.

Other things to look for are the oil/water heat exchanger and the water pump. 
Symptoms of the former's failure are oil in the coolant (looks scummy in the
reservoir) and water in the oil (foam or taffy-coloured goop on the dipstick). 
Symptoms of the water pump include coolant leak, overheating, and noise.  The
exhaust-side motor mount has been upgraded many times (five or six).  Symptoms
include loud, macho growling before you get the revs above 1200-1500 RPM.  The
real test is to measure its height; a bad mount has lost its fluid and
collapsed.

The aftermarket chips of choice are the Autothority version 2.5.  Avoid like the
fiscal plague any kit which drills the recycling valve or shims the waste gate. 
Doing so defeats your DME's ability to protect against overboost, and can mess
up your engine.

To answer one question directly: it's a mistake to buy any car which has been
driven hard when a creampuff can be had for the same or less money.  Last I
checked, dealers weren't interested in paying book (wholesale) for 944-series
cars.  There are so many on the market: many stock brokers find their Porsche's
interior clashes with their new Burger King uniform.  Find yourself one in
better condition.  And by all means have an experienced Porsche mechanic check
it out.

And join the Porsche Club of America!

Hope this helps... Kevin Gross

Jim,

Just to add to Kevin and Patty's good info, another documented 951
gotcha has been the failure of the #2 connecting rod bearing in cars
operated at full throttle for significant periods of time (i.e.,
tracked) running at power outputs above stock via chips.  This problem
is discussed by Bob Strange (B. F. Goodrich test engineer) and Charlie
Learoyd (PCA tech committee member) in the May 1990 issue of Panorama
(page 38).  I doubt that the autocrossing you intend to do with the
car would exacerbate this problem (since you wouldn't be standing on
the gas for long periods), but I just thought someone should mention
it anyway.

If you can get your hands on the February 1990 issue of VW&Porsche
magazine, there is a survey article on the various 951 chips.  The
July 1990 issue of Panorama also contains a good discussion (complete
with an interesting history of Porsche factory-produced chips for the
Turbo Cup series and the famous 300hp "head gasket" chip) on page 61.
The #2 rod bearing problem is mentioned there as well, and the author
(Charlie Learoyd) concurs with Kevin's advice to avoid mucking
mechanically with wastegates and cycling valves (i.e., let the
computers handle the job).  I have not yet bought a chip for my 951,
but if I were to do so I would probably go with the Autothority
product, which I have heard good things about (in addition to Kevin's
recommendation).

My experience with owning an '84 944 and an '86 951 is that oil leaks
and failing water pumps (this is actually a seal problem as well) are
a fact of life with these cars.  My 951 has had an oil leak at the
back of the cam housing for some time now.  Since it doesn't seem to
be causing any problems other than putting a few drops of oil on the
garage floor, I am waiting until it's time to change the cam belt
again, at which point I will pull the housing and replace the gaskets.
I would not, however, similarly delay replacing oil seals on the front
of the engine because of the risk of getting oil on the cam belt.  If
you are planning on doing your own work on the car, you may as well
budget an additional $400 for a belt tensioning tool, since doing damn
near anything on the front of the engine (like resealing the shafts
and installing new water pumps) requires removing the cam and balance
shaft belts.

Good luck in your search for a 951, but don't buy the first one
you see.  There seem to be many good specimens out there for
prices similar to those you quoted.

-Milo Dorr
dorr@crg.llnl.gov

You can get these from Porsche, as part of a "lock kit".  I had my
914 re-keyed back to the original when I had my door handles replaced.

you can't get the pins on a onesie-twosie basis.

that there is a similar part for the 914. I even have the 911 part number
laying about somewhere -- I seem to recall that the kit is about $200,
whereas "doing it the hard way"  (i.e. ordering matched parts for all of
the stuff with locks) is about $800.

Kevin and Milo give great advice!  Last but not least, here's my two cents (plus
tax):

The 86 951 has had two fuel-line related recalls.  One is for one of the hoses
going from the fuel rail through the F/R wheel housing, and the other is a 5"
stub at the front of the rail.  It'd be hard to describe what the fix looks
like; find a local PCA/PCNA person and/or get full records.

The problem with the exhaust is that the solid manifold cracks due to thermal
stress between #1 and #4.  The replacement has an expansion coupling in the #1
pipe.  The ceramic port liners are perfect; there is no problem related to them.

If you run the car on racetracks, you must alter the brakes.  Run with a harder
compound such as Ferodo DS-11, or carbon fiber pads.  Do *NOT* run with stock
pads or you can kiss the front rotors goodbye when you begin driving fast and
hard (they'll be fine at 70% driving but warp as you approach the limit).  You
will also have to change your fluid to a high dry-temperature type, such as
AP-550, available through the mail.  Standard fluids (Castrol DOT 4 LMA) can't
stand the heat; they boil and your pedal goes away.  The addition of a "cool
brake" kit is also a good idea.  It's a system that rams high pressure air into
the rotors.  It's not very good quality and it's expensive ($200), but you
should have it.  Check the cars you're looking at to insure the rotors are OK -
the car should brake smoothly and without shuddering, and the rotors must not
have any radial hairline cracks.

Note that with brake fluid you're looking for a high dry boiling point - the wet
boiling point is insignificant for competition use.  LMA is DOT 4 due to it's
high wet & dry numbers, but AP-550 is DOT 3, since it has a poorer wet boiling
point.  AP-550 is also good for street use.  The wet boiling point doesn't
matter if you bleed the brakes a couple times a year, or before every track
event.

As to the subject of the balance shaft belt and cam belt: the cam belt is
critical to the life of the engine!  Equally important are the pullies that the
belts ride on; Porsche recently issued a service bulletin suggesting replacement
at 45K for the pulley.  These things fail internally and the belt goes limp,
causing extensive internal engine damage.  Have the belt tension checked yearly,
and replace it every 2-4 years along with the pullies (depending on how hard
you'll be driving).

The balance shaft belt is less critical than the cam belt.  If it breaks, the
engine gets rough but that's it.

I've heard of several cases of leaky balance shaft seals and such; this seams to
be a fact of life with these engines.  I've been lucky in that regard, despite
56K miles on an '86, and about 5K track miles.

The oil leak at the back of the cam housing (and probably yours, Milo) is easy
to fix.  Mine and a friend's had that problem.  There are 3 bolts holding the
end-cap on; these are under-torqued by the factory and back out.  Just tighten
them snugly, but not too tight or you'll over-compress the gasket.  If the bolts
are gone, that's a problem too - go buy a new one!  Two of the bolts are easy to
get to but the third's a little trickier.  It's no real problem though.  Nuf
said.

Kevin's comment on the oil/water intercooler is valuable; a lot of those things
failed and were misdiagnosed as blown head gaskets.  It's not hard to get that
fixed.  Same deal with water pumps, a long-standing problem with all water
cooled cars in the world (except Japanese ones...!).

I also endorse the Autothority 2.5 chips, with some caveats.  The Autothority
people tend to experiment carelessly in my opinion; the results can be greatly
reduced engine life.  I've had numerous extended conversions with Auto. people
in person, and have quizzed them on several topics.  Sometimes their responses
were flippant and suggested that they knew exactly what the Whole Deal was,
where Porsche and Bosch were in left field.  Yah, that's why their previous
version of firmware let my engine knock, and the **BAD** drilled cycling valve
idea a few years ago blew many an engine (mine included).

That said, the 2.5 chips are really great, since they include 4 years of
learning from the field testing of earlier versions.  As Kevin said, don't touch
the cycling valve and leave the turbo and wastegate alone!  One nice little
touch is to not use their fixed-size orifice in the vacuum/boost line behind the
air filter housing.  Instead, use a small, high quality valve.  This will give
you complete control over how much boost you get.  I turn mine up for
autocrosses and down for track use.  I limit to 1.8 bar for track and 1.9 for
autocross; this gives around 260 and 270 hp, respectively.  Any higher and
you're running out of safety room on the engine.  A hardware store stocked the
valve I used.

As a side note to engine power increases: Bob Strange told me at Tech Tactics
that the difference in lap time between 247 and 300 hp at Road Atlanta was about
1 second.  Power doesn't gain as much as you'd think; driving on the friction
circle does.

The #2 conrod bearing will die in about 60 track hours.  If you don't replace it
before then, you'll buy an engine.  A guy in CT blew a hole out his block at
Lime Rock because of it.  Absolutely, use Mobil 1 15W-50 oil!  It's very
expensive, but it protects your engine a bit better than conventional oils at
high temperatures and high shear rates (RPM's).  The turbo center bearing will
love you too.

Speaking of the turbocharger, some of them have failed.  One friend had a blade
fall off, and had only 1.2 bar boost.  Another friend had an undetermined
failure, but it still gave him boost.  Mine is whining, making little mechanical
noises, and blows oil into the intake runners and manifold.  If you're about to
purchase a car, pull the intake hose at the throttle plate; look around for oil.
 Also listen for a "distant police siren" when the turbo spools up at low speeds
with the driver's window open.  This sound is distinct from the normal turbo
whistle, but hard to hear.

And of course there's the clutch.  The guide sleeve, an $18 aluminum part, wears
and causes clutch shuddering during engagement.  If it's left uncorrected, the
clutch eventually fails.  Unfortunately, replacing it costs close to $2000 in
labor, and you should replace the clutch at the same time, so add another $1000.
It's possible to save money on parts by finding a discount source, but I
strongly recommend using only factory-blessed parts through the PCNA channels. 
There are many so-called "identical" parts from the same manufacturer, and with
the same part number, that are *not* in fact identical replacement parts.  You
mentioned that the early 951 had this work done; that's a big bonus in my book!

(Note: Clair Motors in West Roxbury, MA gives %15 discount - call Mack in
wholesale parts at 617-323-4600, and mention my name - Jim Selders).

One last item to note is the warranty.  '86 models have a 5/50 warranty on the
engine and drivetrain.  No other year has that.  If you're looking at cars in
the low-mid 40K mile range, you'll still have time left.  This can save your
wallet if those weak '86 parts fall off the car!

I don't know what the market's like in your area, but here in Boston an early
'86 951 would go for more like $17K in average shape.  Deduct for the messed up
front-end of the one car.  $20K is too high.

>From memory, options in 1986 were sport seats, sunroof, sport shocks, LSD,
cruise, metallic paint, forged wheels, rear wiper, central locking, alarm,
extended steering wheel hub, smaller steering wheel, and stereo.  '86's had
transmission coolers and headlamp washers, which became options in '87.  Also,
for cars produced before 9/1/85, there's no CHMSL (third brake light).

Good luck on your purchase.  Feel free to inquire about any other subjects.  If
you'd like a more terse answer, let me know that too!

                JBS

Subject: Re: 911 Torsion Bars
Message-Id: <24901004213742/0003844665NB3EM@mcimail.com>

Torsion springs have splines on them, which mate with the car and determine your
ride height.  If one side doesn't match the other's height, then the springs
were put back "off-by-one" tooth (or possibly more than one).  Unless anyone has
a better idea, I think you have to have the shop pull the two errant bars and
install them again, correctly.  Which two are wrong depends on what ride height
you want.

The ride height should be equal at all corners with a full tank of gas and you
sitting in the driver's seat.

The weight on each wheel is set by the car's design.  Raising the corner 1"
doesn't transfer weight; it just raises the car.  The suspension will, however,
be at a different point and have different camber, toe, etc., so this will
affect handling.

                JBS

Subject: Re:  911 Torsion Bars
Cc: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu

Adjusting the torsion bars is not very hard. You just have to follow the
Porsche Manual procedures. Level the car, then measure the angle. If they
are off, you will have to pull them out and turn inner to right or outers to left (or opposite) to get the right angle. The inner splines are offset
compared to the outers, therefore you can get them within a few minutes
accurate. You will however have to do an allignment after, as your rear
camber and toe will be off, since you have to remove the bolts to pull
the bars. If they are corroded, sand and paint them and put grease
on the bars when you re-install them. If your springplate bushings show
any wear, I recommend you change them at that time. Use Weltmeister inner and
outer bushings on each side, as you cannot get factory replacements without
buying the springplates. Good luck!

Dr. Ing. Karl Frank Hens

1. '951' is the factory project number for the 944 Turbo.  You'll also find it
stamped on many of the car's parts, as the lead clause in the part number.

2. To join the PCA, call Myra Sutton in Richardson, TX, at 214 690 6205.  She
will hook you up with the closest Region (aka chapter), and send you an
application form and a sample issue of 'Panorama', our national monthly.  Your
local region membership secretary will send you a copy of the region's
newsletter, listing their calendar upcoming events.

3. Autothority numbers:
Sales: 703 323 0919
Service: 703 323 7830
FAX: 703 323 7325

4. An autocross is a driving event, the basic idea of which is to negotiate a
course marked out with pylons as quickly as possible without knocking any pylons
over or out of position.  Cars are grouped into classes of similar performance:
either the car's or the driver's, depending on local convention.  You need a
helmet and you car will be checked for mechanical soundness.  Tons of fun!

Kevin Gross

From: "F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 201 949 6348" <homxc!fwk@rutgers.edu>

Subject: 914s

	
	I always always always use 20/50W in air cooled engines. I like Kendall and 

In my 914 I used 15W-40 Valvoline Allfleet(sic?), in an attempt to minimize
the viscosity range. 20W-50 is ok if you seldom drive the car in cold
weather (e.g. below freezing).
	
		
		I am interested in making it a FUN car, and not necessarily an
		all-original car.  I'm excited about it!
	
	Get Bilstein shocks, Yoko tires, the biggest sway bar you can find and
	
I prefer Koni's, but Bilsteins are certianly reasonable. Yokohama makes
a wide range of tires- if you want "boy racer" tires, A008RTUs are
among the best (except for the well known wet weather issue), with the
other candidate being BFG Comp T/A R1. However, for street use many would
find that the additional handling is not worth the terrible lifetime.
If you want a good street tire with a different balance of wear, stick
and wet safety from the boy racer tires, I am not sure that Yokohamas
are the best choice anymore. I would consider Bridgestone RE71s or
Comp-T/A (not the R) as the high cost, high overall performance choices.
Others may want to comment on the "value" choices.
	
I have a strong comment about very stiff sway bars on an otherwise
unmodified 914. The chassis is simply not stiff enough without a roll
cage or at least significant additional stiffening underneath to
support a stiff suspension. The fastest 914s without roll cages
at PCA track events have suspensions near the factory optional setup.
Either buy the factory bars if you can find them, or buy an aftermarket
front bar like the H+H 19 mm, and run it on the softest or near
the softest setting. Dont neglect the back bar, where the factory bar
with the stock springs is the best setup. Another alternative is
to use 140 lb (but not stiffer) rear springs without a bar.

In any case, if you are willing to compromise some wear for handling,
dial in more negative camber, perhaps -1 deg. front and -1.5 deg. rear
or, if you are really gung-ho, perhaps 1.5 deg. front and 2 deg
or even 2.5 deg. in the rear. At the latter setting, the car won't track
very nicely in a straight line.

Frank Kerfoot
!att!homxc!fwk

[ saga of '70 911T high on the HCs ]

Two suggestions:
1) Advance the timing.  Advance it as far as you're comfy and as far
as you can and still be able to start the car.

2) Crank the idle speed up to the allowable maximum.  I can't figure
out what state you're in from the address, but CA has higher
allowable idle speeds for older cars.  Your local smog station won't
go above the 1150 or whatever it is, but the BAR (Bureau of Auto
Repair (down the street from the Ministry of Love and around the
corner from the Commission for Oral Sex)) will.

Doing these two things will produce more complete combustion in the
cylinder.  Basically, they do the same thing: advance the timing.  It
means that the time between spark and exhaust is a little longer.  It
also means that you'll get more NOx in the exhaust, but '70 cars are
still in the NOx exempt era, and you're gonna retune your car
afterwards, anyway, so it shouldn't matter.

Hope this helps.

andy

From porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu  Fri Oct  5 09:32:32 1990

Original-From: homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 201 949 6348)

Subject: weight balancing

	were put back "off-by-one" tooth (or possibly more than one).  Unless anyone has

With Porsche torsion bars, you cna be off by much less than one tooth.
They use a "verneer" system, where the number of teeth at one end of
the bar is different than the number at the other. You can meve very
small angles by moving one tooth at one end, and one tooth the other way
at the opposite end. This is part of what makes the whole process 
"interesting".

	
	The ride height should be equal at all corners with a full tank of gas and you
	sitting in the driver's seat.
	
	The weight on each wheel is set by the car's design.  Raising the corner 1"
	doesn't transfer weight; it just raises the car.  The suspension will, however,
	be at a different point and have different camber, toe, etc., so this will
	affect handling.
	
	
Actually, raising one wheel will affect the relative weights. You can't
change the weight on the sum of the front wheels, or the sum on the
left side (and similarly the back and the right side), but you can
change the diagonal ratios. I therefore question the people who claim
to get balance within a few pounds left to right, with an inherently
assymetric car (driver not in center). You can get the back two wheels
equal, or you can get the front two wheels equal, but not both.
I'm sure we can spend megabytes deciding which of the above is preferred
(I try for achieving the same front/back weight ratio on each side of the
car).

When you get the weights right, the ride heights at the four corners
may or may not be uniform. My experience is that usually they are close.

Your observation that ride height changes affect suspension settings like
camber is accurate. Camber then affects relative weights, so it
is very much an iterative process to get the combination of suspension
settings and wheel weights you want.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

It definitely sounds like your right side torsion springs are lower than they
should be, and that this happened when your mechanic reinstalled them after
replacing the bushings.  The correct fix is to have them put in properly.

Don't "patch" the problem with the sway bar; this will give you drastically
different handling in right vs. left turns.  It's not the sway bar's function to
level the car!  You'll kill the sway bar's bushings and have a weird handling
vehicle to boot.

If the torsion springs are indeed mounted correctly, then there must be another
problem somewhere.  I suggest you find the *real* problem instead of mucking
around with a pseudo-fix.  Good luck!

                JBS

To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
In-Reply-To: Jim Selders's message of Thu, 4 Oct 90 16:37 EST <24901004213742/0003844665NB3EM@mcimail.com>
Subject: 911 Torsion Bars


OK, forgive me, I have a tendency of posting too rapidly since I
rarely have much time.  THe point I wanted to make is that the ride
height, expecially in older cars, of the fenders is not the thing to
adjust.  It's the weight distribution on the tires.  It may very well
be that Jeffrey's car's problem is that the torsion bar was installed
wrong.  I was merely reacting to what is a common occurrence amongst
911 and 914 owners, since the front is so easy to lower, often you see
people trying to adjust all four corners to the same height.  This is
wrong, although often you get the correct results.

I see a couple responses and they reflect some knowledge of the
issue.  Let me try to give a better explanation, I think my quick shot
was probably too ambiguous, and I don't think I said what I meant.

> Return-Path: <porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu> Date: Thu, 4 Oct 90
> 16:37 EST From: Jim Selders <0003844665@mcimail.com>
>
> The ride height should be equal at all corners with a full tank of gas
> and you sitting in the driver's seat.

This is only true of new cars, that is the way they are designed.
Often, as time goes on, uneven wear on the springs may cause slight
differences.  As a result, adjusting the ride height to equality on
all four corners could very well be making the problem worse.

> The weight on each wheel is set by the car's design.  Raising the
> corner 1" doesn't transfer weight; it just raises the car.  The
> suspension will, however, be at a different point and have different
> camber, toe, etc., so this will affect handling.

Raising the corner (that is, raising the height of the fender) doesn't
transfer weight, it INCREASES the load on that wheel and on the
diagonally opposite wheel (note - a very common misconception is that
by increasing load on one wheel you decrease load on the diagonally
opposite, but the truth is they BOTH increase).  The "car's design" in
the case of a 911 is for the wheels to have a similar load side to side.

> Return-Path: <porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu> From:
> fwk@homxc.att.com Date: Fri, 5 Oct 90 09:37 EDT Original-From:
> homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 201 949 6348)
> 	
> Actually, raising one wheel will affect the relative weights. You can't
> change the weight on the sum of the front wheels, or the sum on the
> left side (and similarly the back and the right side), but you can
> change the diagonal ratios.

OK, close, but not exactly (It seems like you know the right thing to
do but not why, or perhaps you're having the same problem as I did in
my last posting, not expressing what you mean).  You actually can
change the weight on each wheel.  The easiest way to explain what is
actually a very difficult problem is by thinking of a table resting
evenly on four legs.  If you were to raise one leg, most of the weight
of the table would then be supported by that leg and the diagonal one,
and the table would teeter between the other two.  Thus, raising the
fender (which is really lowering the wheel) will increase the total
weight supported by the diagonal pair of wheels.

>  I therefore question the people who claim
> to get balance within a few pounds left to right, with an inherently
> assymetric car (driver not in center). You can get the back two wheels
> equal, or you can get the front two wheels equal, but not both.
> I'm sure we can spend megabytes deciding which of the above is preferred
> (I try for achieving the same front/back weight ratio on each side of the
> car).

Most cars are pretty symmetrical (l-r).  In fact the left-right weight
difference in a 911 with just a driver is under 1%, and with a
passenger is about 4% (that's percent of total car weight), which
isn't that much.  Your method is correct, the proper procedure for
adjusting weight balance is called "corner balancing".  The idea is to
maintain the same ratio of front/back tire weights on each side.  When
this is done, the car no longer "teeters" the way the table would if
you raised one leg.  This teetering is an extremely dangerous
performance characteristic.  Once the ratios are correct, usually the
job is complete, for now the left and right front tires should have
within 20 lbs of weight on them.  If not, then you have the equivalent
of a table with two adjacent legs raised - it won't "teeter", in fact
it will be stable, but it will be tilted.  In this case, you have more weight
on one side than the other.  This isn't bad, and apparently some
people are satisfied with this, but the problem remains that in hard
braking, the lighter side will lock up sooner than the heavier.  To
fix this is really complicated, because while corner balancing can be done
by just adjusting the front torsion bars (which is easy), side to side
balancing requires adjusting all four and it takes an ENOURMOUS amount
of time.   It's much easier to just buy new springs that are fresh.

I personally have corner balanced a few cars, and they've all come out
within 10 lbs right-left difference (w/driver).  That they come out
looking lopsided sometimes is a fact that can be corrected with new
springs, but it's really not necesary.

THis information is from my memory of:
  Carrol Smith's book "Tune to Win" (I think that was the name)
  An article in Pano last year, and one in one of the Upfixen books.
  A phone call with Bruce Anderson.

> Your observation that ride height changes affect suspension settings
> like camber is accurate. Camber then affects relative weights, so it
> is very much an iterative process to get the combination of suspension
> settings and wheel weights you want.

Generally should have your car realigned after changing the balance.
Obviously if you pull the rear torsion bars you must.

Shit, I already spent a half hour on this - sorry for spelling
mistakes, hope this helps some...

From: fwk@homxc.att.com

Original-From: homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 201 949 6348)
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Weight Balance

	> Actually, raising one wheel will affect the relative weights. You can't
	> change the weight on the sum of the front wheels, or the sum on the
	> left side (and similarly the back and the right side), but you can
	> change the diagonal ratios.
	
	OK, close, but not exactly (It seems like you know the right thing to
	do but not why, or perhaps you're having the same problem as I did in
	my last posting, not expressing what you mean).  You actually can
	change the weight on each wheel.  The easiest way to explain what is
	actually a very difficult problem is by thinking of a table resting
	evenly on four legs.  If you were to raise one leg, most of the weight
	of the table would then be supported by that leg and the diagonal one,
	and the table would teeter between the other two.  Thus, raising the
	fender (which is really lowering the wheel) will increase the total
	weight supported by the diagonal pair of wheels.

I agree with what you are saying here, but I believe that it agrees with
rather than disputes my hypothesis that without changing the angle of the
table (or car), you cant change the SUM of the weights on any pair of
ADJACENT legs (or wheels), but you can change the sum  of the
diagonals (my previous posting referred to "diagonal ratios", and that
is in fact a poor way to express it).
	
	>  I therefore question the people who claim
	> to get balance within a few pounds left to right, with an inherently
	> assymetric car (driver not in center). You can get the back two wheels
	> equal, or you can get the front two wheels equal, but not both.
	> I'm sure we can spend megabytes deciding which of the above is preferred
	> (I try for achieving the same front/back weight ratio on each side of the
	> car).
	
	Most cars are pretty symmetrical (l-r).  In fact the left-right weight
	difference in a 911 with just a driver is under 1%, and with a
	passenger is about 4% (that's percent of total car weight), which

My experience (911s, 914s, several symetrical and asymetrical race cars)
is that most cars have reasonably symetrical weights without the driver,
since there is not nuch to create assymetry (perhaps the battery, etc).
I'm not aware of any Porsche which has a designed in offset of the
drivetrain. Assuming that is the case, adding a 200# driver (my case)
puts ~100# more on the left side (since the driver doesn't sit directly
over the left wheels). If you use the rule of even left-right ratios,
this would put ~40# extra on the LF and ~60# on the LR, not far from
your 20# value. Others would argue that in a 911, the backs should be
exactly equal, and then the fronts get the whole 100# differential.
A lighter driver would help (no volunteers please!).

	maintain the same ratio of front/back tire weights on each side.  When
	this is done, the car no longer "teeters" the way the table would if
	you raised one leg.  This teetering is an extremely dangerous
	performance characteristic.  Once the ratios are correct, usually the
	job is complete, for now the left and right front tires should have
	within 20 lbs of weight on them.  If not, then you have the equivalent
	of a table with two adjacent legs raised - it won't "teeter", in fact

I absolutely agree that you want to eliminate "teetering", but in my
experience there is a range of settings which meet that criterion
due to inherent assymetry with a driver in the car.
	
	
	> Your observation that ride height changes affect suspension settings
	> like camber is accurate. Camber then affects relative weights, so it
	> is very much an iterative process to get the combination of suspension
	> settings and wheel weights you want.
	
	Generally should have your car realigned after changing the balance.
	Obviously if you pull the rear torsion bars you must.

I think you missed my point. When you align the car, you will (or can)
screw up the weight balance, requiring an iterative procedure. Toe
changes are obviously ok, but camber changes will change the weights somewhat.

	
Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

Subject: Re: 911 Torsion Bars

Nice piece, Chris.  It was very informative.

I think there might be a misuse of words or definitions that's bothering me in
this dialog.  I'm thinking of my physics classes when you talk about changing a
corner's weight by altering its height.  In the pure sense, this isn't possible
(assuming the car stays fairly close to earth, etc.).

Is what you mean, that when one wheel (for instance) is lower than others, the
dynamic load is greater?  That is intuitively correct to me.  However, the
weight, or static load, remains identical.

For example, if both front wheels hit a speed bump at the same moment, then on
the one that's lower (higher chassis ride height), the spring/shock will begin
resisting the bump shortly before the other front side.  This is because the
diagonal corner's suspension doesn't want to be "teetered" into the ground by
the lowered wheel/raised chassis, so it's doing it's Newtonian thing and pushing
back, thus increasing the force at the lower front wheel.

Hmmmm, now that I think about it, this'll increase the apparent force on that
front wheel even statically.  But it's still due to the diagonal corner fighting
back - the *weight* of the corner is still the same.

Hey, it's Friday night and I'm going to go play with fast cars from Germany!

                JBS

	OK, guys, if you're going to tell tall stories about $50 Porsches, 
I've got a *true* story for you. I got my black 1990 911 Carrera 2 Cabrio
for $A50, which is about $US42. That was the price of the ticket in the 
lottery I won! (My wife prefers to tell friends that "one of our investments 
came good").
	The magnitude of my good fortune may not be apparent to US Porsche
owners, so here's some background. Thanks to what you might describe as
extremely aggressive taxing of luxury cars, the list price of this car in 
Australia is $A175000 (say $US140000). Now, I'm not particularly poorly paid
(20 years experience, network manager at a large tertiary education
institution), but this represents something over 6 years of my post-tax income.
Or, it represents around 1.6 times the price of a typical new house and block
of land here in Adelaide, which is a State capital. In other words, it's not
the sort of car that is commonly owned by your average Computer Centre person.
My two Civics and an Accord are more representative of what we see in the car
park. This does mean that new Porsches are very rare, so there is a lot of
"head-turning" value in it!
	I've just (two hours ago) arrived home after the last 800-mile leg of 
a trip to Newcastle (near Sydney) in the car. When I've had some sleep and 
cleaned up 191 new mail messages, I might write an article about the car and 
the trip. In the mean time, Marciano (and "JZ") can quietly grind their teeth!
Perhaps a little teaser: 2200 miles, mostly at 70-80 mph, saw perhaps 6 police 
cars, and of course didn't get booked. 

                                    Rollo Ross, Network Manager

Subject: upgrading stock 944 suspension

Ok, let's say we have this guy who wants to upgrade his stock 944 suspension.
He gets on some Porsche snailmail lists, and he starts getting Porsche
catalogs. Well one of these catalogs has this nice multi-step process
for upgrading suspension. The catalog in question is Performance Products.
Our man takes a gander at the catalog, and here is what he comes up with:

STEP 1: Koni adjustable shocks, 1038Ts for the back at $158.95 per. 1209Ts
	for the front at $96.50 per. Total price of STEP 1: $510.90

STEP 2: 25mm Torsion bars for $241.00 per pair. Stock height 220lbs springs
	for $75.50. Total price of STEP 2: $316.50

STEP 3: Weltmeister sway bars, front 28mm kit $329.50, rear 22mm kit $214.50,
	plus adapter kit since this 944 has no factory rear bar, $68.50.
	Total price of STEP 3: $612.50

Total price of STEPS 1-3: $1439.90 for parts.

So this fellow is wondering, indeed asking the experts out there: Is it worth
it to do this to a 1984 944 with 72000 miles? Remember that this car has
a new set of RE71 tires and alloy 3 piece wheels, the existing shocks are
the originals, and the driver of the car wants to join his friends out on
the local autocross courses.

Also, let's say this guy made some money by selling short in the recent
stock market woes, so money is less of an object, but doing things right
is the object and is very important...

Any friendly advice for this person would be greatly appreciated. I will
be sure to pass the info along to him :-)...

Subject: Re: upgrading stock 944 suspension

Steve, let me respond to your 944 suspension question more with philosophy than
hard yes/no's.

Porsche does a fairly good job with the suspension design.  In particular, they
choose spring and shock rates that are harmonious.  That is, they work very well
together and are well integrated.  Sometimes it's too soft for what you want,
though, so you wanna spend $$$ to get it firmer/stiffer/faster etc.

There are many, many companies willing to sell you a part to stiffen up things,
but it won't be matched with other components.  What you get is a slightly
faster and firmer car, but without the great factory tuning.  Don't sell them
short in Weissach; they do a lot of work to get things right.

The Koni adjustable shocks are nice.  Adjustablility is good so you can tune
them a bit.  But this is probably jounce rate only, I'd guess.  The rebound is
set by design.  Those two rates determine a shock's characteristics.  You may
also want to consider Porsche OEM sport shocks so you're assured they're matched
well with your spring rates and chassis weight.

The springs will make the car better on smooth surfaces but much less
comfortable on bumpy (i.e., real) roads.  They'll transmit forces into the
chassis faster, which has an effect on chassis, bushing, and trim wear.  You'll
also get less roll with stiffer springs.

The sway bars are, in my opinion, the best deal of the lot: they keep the car
flat, and easy to control and tune, but don't affect the ride or comfort beyond
that.  The '84 needs much more roll stiffness but only a little firmer ride to
my posterior's way of thinking.

Also, have you considered updating the front control arms or rear trailing arms
to the 85 1/2 aluminum ones?  They're lighter and stronger.  The stamped steel
front arms have been known to fatigue and fail.  Some track people around here
have updated the arms purely from the saftey standpoint.  When I changed from my
'83 944 to my '86 951, the suspension was *notably* more compliant and smooth. 
The unsprung weight seems to be down.  Anyway, I thought I'd mention that
because it's a subtle but nice change.

I've heard through the years a couple important messages from people far more
experienced than I, and they seem to be very accurate.  First, making a car
stiffer and spending loads of money on it doesn't get a whole lot back (except
possibly for the very advanced driver).  Second, Porsche does a lot of research
and development on suspensions and has decades of experience tuning race and
street cars.  They know more than we ever will!

Along those lines, I've kept my own modifications very simply and I've been
highly pleased.  My 951 has a totally base suspension except for fat Weltmeister
bars tuned for a slight power-off understeer, power-on neutral attitude.  That
coupled with 225/245 Yokohama A008R's has made a nearly unbeatable combination
of handling.  I love the car set up this way, and I can tune it for different
tires fairly easily.  The bars made every difference, and the car is still
entirely useable on the street.  A friend with basically the same car but a full
lowered, stiffened suspension has a car that's horrible to drive in and squeaks
terribly, plus he's slower on the autocross course!

In summary, what I'm saying here is to repeat what I've heard for years and
seems to make perfect sense: keep the modifications simple and just learn to
drive better.  That's really the key!  Buy or borrow a G-Analyst and get
yourself out on the friction circle, whether it be at .8 or 1.2 G's.  Let me
know what you end up doing and how it works!

                JBS

My two bits:  Flip #2 and #3, go for the sway bars first.  And if you want to be
competitive at the autocross, get some R-compound sticky tires, like Yoko A008R
or A008RTU, BFG Comp T/A R, Bridgestone RE71R, etc.

Two other recommendations.  First, don't wait for any of the above before
autocrossing.  The one thing which needs the most work and will yield the
biggest improvement in times is the driver.  Get out and enjoy yourself! 
Second, call me next time you have an inspiration regarding the stock market!

Kevin

Subject: 944 suspension upgrades


Tell your "friend" that all of the parts you listed are good quality
and will make the car handle better. They will also make the car 
significantly stiffer on the street, which some people would find
uncomfortable (but which others either tolerate or like). Several of
the parts you listed can be bought from other aftermarket suppliers
for significantly less money, if you care. If you are going with
that type of setup, you may want to consider the Koni "S" series,
which standf for sport (typically the same or almost the same price,
and the same number followed by an S).

Another variable, and perhaps the one with the most bang for the buck,
is tires. A set of "boy racer" tires like A008RTU or Comp T/A R1
will amaze you (both with their stick and their rapid wear).

More broadly, however, I would suggest that you just go out and drive
some autocrosses or whatever. A stock 944 is a fine car right out of the
box.  Leave it alone and buy seat time, undil you develop some driver
skill in that environment, then fiddle with the car if you want.
If your 944 doesn't have the optional sway bars you might want to consider
that (actually, you said you need the adapter for the Weltmeister rear,
so I assume you don't have the optional bars). Buy a set of stock
optional bars from a Porsche wrecker like Kempton (my recollection on
bars is $85 per end). The car will still be very nice on the street.
Make it a race car later, when you are surer that is what you want.
(or what your "friend" wants- sorry) If you are already sure, dive
in and have fun.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

Subject: 009 distributor, Solexes, Webers

009 distributor, Solexes, Webers
> hi - I'm new to this list, BTW...  just bought my '69 912 (red, alloys,
> 1750 kit, 009 dist., no rust, etc.) an really like it.

I'm new to the list, also.  Perhaps one of the more experienced 356/912 owners
would comment on the use of 009 distributors.  My 356C also has one, but
I understand that it has less advance than the 050 distributor that should
have been on the engine.  What should the timing be set to for the 009?
Should I find an 050 and replace the 009?

> I've got a few questions I'd like to ask... (911 guys listen, too)

> 2. Mechanic says my Solex's (split shafts BTW) are weeping gas, both down
>   the throat and outside.  Is it worth rebuilding them (can I do them
>   myself - I've done Strombergs and Holley's before???) or should I
>   throw 'em out and buy Webers (manifold or new adaptors - I'd lean
>   toward adaptors, else - I'm told - I'd have to pull the engine to
>    hook 'em up)??  Mechanic likes Webers - says that when you adjust 'em
>   they stay there and don't change.  Are Webers legal for CA smog????

You might try tightening the screws on top of the Solexes.  My Zeniths
were leaking, and that solved the problem.  As for rebuilding them, it
primarily depends on the condition of the throttle shaft.  If there is
any appreciable wear, vacuum leaks will cause problems.  As far as
Webers go, be careful - they don't come set up correctly, and need to
be rejetted.  Ask your mechanic for references - find another 356/912
owner that has had the work done by him.  I looked at five 912's when
I was searching for my Porsche, and only one was correctly jetted.
Another problem I seem to recall is that the Webers come with a
smaller venturi than the Solexes, and a performance decrease can
actually result if used as is.  Perhaps someone with more knowledge of
the problem can confirm/deny this.  The owners of the cars that had
Webers said they are legal, but they had to braze a connection for
the oil-breather hose onto the air-cleaner in order to pass smog.  One
owner had a letter from DMV certifying the legality of the swap.

I have heard del'Orto carbs come highly recommended as Solex replacements.

BTW, pulling the engine is relatively simple - you will have to
disconnect fuel and accelerator linkage anyhow - all that remains are
the electrical connections, the cables and ducts for the heater boxes,
possibly the rear pan (sheet metal), the tach cable (or do 912s have an
electronic tach?) and four bolts that attach the engine to the transmission.  
At most, it is an additional 1.5 hours work to pull it and reinstall it.

-Dave Erickson

	hi - I'm new to this list, BTW...  just bought my '69 912 (red, alloys,

Buy yourself a Bosch 050 distributor.....the advance curve is much better
for the 356/912 motor. The 009 works, but you'll like the 050 better.....
	
	I've got a few questions I'd like to ask... (911 guys listen, too)
	
	1. (I know, a REAL old Porsche is one from the 40's or 50's,
	    but still, mine isn't BRAND NEW).

Your Porsche is beautiful.....wish I had one....my '61 356 is wonderful, 
but a 912 to drive every day would be too much fun for one human to have....
	
	2. Mechanic says my Solex's (split shafts BTW) are weeping gas, both down
	   the throat and outside.  Is it worth rebuilding them (can I do them
	   myself - I've done Strombergs and Holley's before???) or should I
	   throw 'em out and buy Webers (manifold or new adaptors - I'd lean
	   toward adaptors, else - I'm told - I'd have to pull the engine to
	    hook 'em up)??  Mechanic likes Webers - says that when you adjust 'em
	   they stay there and don't change.  Are Webers legal for CA smog????

Solexes that weep gas down into the intake can ruin your motor! If an intake
valve happens to be open when you're parked with the gas tank higher than
the carb can fill the combustion chamber with gasoline. Simple physics 
sez you can't compress liguids. So you get a bent rod when you try to 
fire that baby up again....called the "gasoline hammer" effect....

You can probably rebuild them, though old solexes can wear in the throttle
shaft thingie and then rebuilding won't help. I have Weber 40IDF carbs and
adapto-plates and they are indeed wonderful! I think, however, that you 
may have to reinstall those solexes at smog time.....call the BAR or
a referee station for confirmation.....(*DON'T* tell them you're thinking
about keeping two sets, of course...) 
	
You can install the webers without dropping your motor. The adapto-plates
come in various flavors, though....and some are better than others. The
best kind are available from Harry Pellow (408) 727-1864 and Charles
at Guaranteed Import Repair (408) 425-8158. Both will gladly ship to 
your address. I believe Charles maintains lower prices...

	3. Best prices on parts?  I have catalogs from Tweeks, (can't remember the
	   rest - about 6 other catalogs came with the car).  Or is it a case of
	   "you have to compare each source for each part because they vary"???
	   Let me hear about any parts houses who have given you hassles (ie.
	   forever backorders, no returns, etc.).

Buy a copy of VW/Porsche or maybe other mags.....they have ads for exclusively 
Porsche junkyards and such......Also, both Charles and Pellow are good sources
for your parts....

JZ

	My 356C also has one, (009) but
	I understand that it has less advance than the 050 distributor that should
	have been on the engine.  What should the timing be set to for the 009?
	Should I find an 050 and replace the 009?

Well, I touched on it in my first reply, but this is essentially the scoop
as I understand it. The 050 has more total advance (like 33 degrees, and the 
022 which was stock [I think] has 35 total degrees) and the advance curve
is more aggressive on the 050 than the 009. 

When timing your porsche, set the timing to 30-35 degrees BTDC at FULL ADVANCE
(i.e. over 3500 rpm) Then check to see where it falls at idle. My 050 gets
33 degrees at full advance and is right at TDC at idle. Works like a 
charm.....
	
	As far as
	Webers go, be careful - they don't come set up correctly, and need to
	be rejetted.  Ask your mechanic for references - find another 356/912
	owner that has had the work done by him. 

Yes! This is true. I went through several jetting changes on my 356 before
I found the trick-hot setup. Charles (at Guaranteed Import Repair) was
great about providing the jets and helping out in this regard. Pellow 
has firm ideas about the right jets, and he ships the webers with the jets
that he likes for your car....I expect that they are close to or the 
same as the ones I settled on.....(I don't quite remember the numbers,
I'd have to go home and look to check...) 

	Another problem I seem to recall is that the Webers come with a
	smaller venturi than the Solexes, and a performance decrease can
	actually result if used as is.  Perhaps someone with more knowledge of
	the problem can confirm/deny this. 

Well, I went from shitty zeniths to Solexes that couldn't be rebuilt to 
New Webers, and I found that the Webers were the best! ;^} Seriously,
though, I can't confirm/deny this statement except to say that the 
Webers seem to have loads of power to me!  Oh yeah! Be sure to get 
Italian made Webers, not Spanish made.....I understand that there
is a Definite quality difference.....

	The owners of the cars that had
	Webers said they are legal, but they had to braze a connection for
	the oil-breather hose onto the air-cleaner in order to pass smog.  One
	owner had a letter from DMV certifying the legality of the swap.
	
If you have 912 "big horn" air cleaners, there should be a place for

	I have heard del'Orto carbs come highly recommended as Solex replacements.
	
Dellortos are almost exact duplicates of the Weber 40IDF, except that they're
about $25 cheaper......

 Well, my mechanic called me early this morning and told
 me my new throttle switch was in. For those of you who
 aren't familiar with the 914 motors they use a Bosch
 fuel injection system with an electromechanical switch
 triggered by the movement of the throttle plate in the
 throttle body. 

 I was having problems cruising at steady rpms and having
 the ignition/fuel injection drop out for a couple of beats.

 I thought it was the trigger points or the ignition points
 and replaced the distributor, trigger points and put in
 an Allison optical trigger to replace the points (no more
 replacing points every 8000 miles, yeah!!).

 This didn't solve the problem so we took the car for a 
 long test drive and when the glitch occurred my mechanic
 said that he thought it was the throttle switch.

 $76 from VW, $60 from Porsche. Porsche seems consistently
 cheapest for parts for this motor (amazingly enough). Same
 part number, different pricing structure.

 Anyway, pulled the throttle valve assembly (the throttle
 switch is mounted to the bottom of the assembly on a 1.7 
 litre motor) and cleaned it thoroughly and put on a new
 switch. Voila -- no more drop-out.

 But that's not really the reason I'm writing. 

 The car is running very well right now after a complete
 tune-up. I thought it was ok yesterday, now it's great.
 We took the throttle valve assembly and tanked it in 
 solvent, brushed off all the crud, blew out the passageways
 and lubed it.

 I would highly recommend this for any similar motor design.
 My idle went up 400rpm after cleaning the assembly and
 the whole throttle mechanism feels like silk. Very smooth
 and seems much more responsive. No hesitation when I press
 the accelerator and a really smooth motion.

 I think this sort of work is very easy and easily overlooked
 and I wanted to recommend it to at least the other 914
 owners out there. The throttle assembly is often somewhat
 neglected and given its position on the motor it is rarely
 if ever cleaned.

 The whole process of removal and cleaning took perhaps
 1/2 hr.

 Stefan

To: 0003844665@mcimail.com
Cc: 0004056890@mcimail.com, porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
In-Reply-To: Jim Selders's message of Fri, 5 Oct 90 17:04 EST <61901005220416/0003844665NB3EM@mcimail.com>
Subject: 911 Torsion Bars


Jim, I've been away for a week so I didn't get a chance to respond.

> I think there might be a misuse of words or definitions that's
> bothering me in this dialog.  I'm thinking of my physics classes when
> you talk about changing a corner's weight by altering its height.  In
> the pure sense, this isn't possible (assuming the car stays fairly
> close to earth, etc.).

I always have trouble relating this to physics.  If there were only
two wheels it would be simple, but with four, it becomes a "statically
indeterminant" problem, it is a highly non-linear function and optomizing
the weights is virtually impossible.  Only existing rules of thumb
will work.  The procedure I outlined is one.

> Is what you mean, that when one wheel (for instance) is lower than
> others, the dynamic load is greater?  That is intuitively correct to
> me.  However, the weight, or static load, remains identical.

First of all, weight is generally "mg" (mass times gravitational
constant), so when I say `weight' of the wheel perhaps I was wrong.  
The car has a weight, which won't change.  Put up on blocks, this
weight is distributed across the car to the four corners in a certain
way, which also can't be changed (well, can't be changed by this
process.  You can put weights there), I'll call this the static weight
distribution.  Note all this applies to a non-moving car only.

In my last msg, I said `weight' for a number of reasons:

- First, weight is often used to refer to the "force pushing down".
When I said weight of the wheel, I meant the downforce exerted by each
wheel on the ground.  Let me call this the static loading on the
wheel.  This is *not* the dynamic load - the dynamic load is a term
usually used to describe the changing load on a wheel while the car is
moving (cornering, aerodynamics, braking, etc).  

- Second, what scales measure is usually called weight.  The static
loading on a wheel can be measured with an ordinary "bathroom" scale,
and when you change the height of one of the front wheels, the reading
on the scale changes.

- Third, the sum of all the scale readings is always constant, and
always adds up to the total weight of the car.

> Hmmmm, now that I think about it, this'll increase the apparent force
> on that front wheel even statically.  But it's still due to the
> diagonal corner fighting back - the *weight* of the corner is still
> the same.

Again, the best way to think about it is a four legged table.  You
obviously can't change the weight of the table by raising one leg, but
you *can* change the distribution of the weight to the legs by raising
one.  Raising one will cause the table to rest on three instead of
four legs, and the weight supported by each leg will change.

In a car, it's far more complex, because raising one corner doesn't
cause another to come completely off the ground.  Also, the static
weight distribution is not constant as it is in the table (at least
the one I was thinking of).  Raising one corner will increase the
amount of static loading on the wheel there (and it's diagonal pair),
meaning a greater percentage of the cars weight is now being supported
by that wheel.  You havn't changed the static weight distribution of
the car, but you have changed the static load on each wheel.  THis can
be seen, as I've said, by putting a scale under each wheel.  Raising
one wheel will increase the scale reading on that wheel and its
opposite, and decrease the scale reading on the other two.  The sum of
the four readings will always remain the same.  [read those last few
sentences a few times if you still have questions, empirical data is
often the proof of the pudding].  There are no springs
fighting back, this is a steady state reading, and it is absolutley a
measure of the portion of the cars weight that wheel is carrying.  The
reason the static load on a wheel can be changed whereas on a table it
can't is because the position of the springs indicate how much they
will support.  If one spring is pushing up more than another, the
counter force (which we all forces have) must be pushing down more.

=====

weltyc@cs.rpi.edu             ...!njin!nyser!weltyc |  seiner schoensten Form"

Subject: Weight Balance


> My experience (911s, 914s, several symetrical and asymetrical race
> cars) is that most cars have reasonably symetrical weights without the
> driver, since there is not nuch to create assymetry (perhaps the
> battery, etc).  I'm not aware of any Porsche which has a designed in
> offset of the drivetrain. Assuming that is the case, adding a 200#
> driver (my case) puts ~100# more on the left side (since the driver
> doesn't sit directly over the left wheels). If you use the rule of
> even left-right ratios, this would put ~40# extra on the LF and ~60#
> on the LR, not far from your 20# value. Others would argue that in a
> 911, the backs should be exactly equal, and then the fronts get the
> whole 100# differential.  A lighter driver would help (no volunteers
> please!).

Well, it's not exact, but things like the oil tank and cooler  are
mounted on the right side just to counter the driver, and I think the
gas tank is designed to carry more on the right..  W/o a driver,
the 911 is definitely heavier on the right side, but by how much I
don't know.  Anyway, the heights can be adjusted so that the two
fronts and two rears are equal, I say this because I've done it.  I
really don't have time to figure out exactly why, but I suppose given
an afternoon and a good enough reason I could write up a free body
diagram and work it out.  Or maybe I could suggest to one of my
colleagues in the physics dept that he put a problem like this on a
test.

glue, or whatever it is, separated but it hasn't separated from the metal
lip that covers the whole top of the glass... 


I've seen a 924 turbo (first or second year) with the glass completely out
of the lip.  That's gotta suck.  Be assured, though, the glass will not
fly out because it's bolted in  on the hatch frame a little below the
hatch shocks.  But this would certainly be a sorry thing for our cars to bear.

From porschephiles-request@bcm.tmc.edu  Sun Oct 14 16:33:10 1990
Received: by wilkins.bcm.tmc.edu (AA24903); Sun, 14 Oct 90 16:33:11 CDT

From: Rollo.Ross@lv.sait.edu.au
Subject: The $50 Porsche in Australia
Sender: Rollo Ross <CCAJR@lv.sait.edu.au>
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Message-Id: <1C8B9616D645E02121@sait.edu.au>
