	Last week I told you about the 911 Cabrio I won with a $A50 lottery 
ticket, and said that the price of this car here is about $US140000. That was 
at the old sales tax rate for luxury cars of 30%. The current tax rate is 50%, 
pushing the price of my Cabrio to around $US160K! Gives you a warm glow to know
that my government got over $US30000 in taxes on my car - plus whatever they
took in import duties.
	My car is a proper built-for-Australia model, which means it's
virtually the same as the US ones with the exception of the right-hand drive
conversion and any minor engine changes due to emissions requirements. (The
Road and Track test in April listed it at 247 HP; my handbook says 250 HP, so
I'm not sure whether there's actually any difference.) The change to right-hand
drive has been done rather half-heartedly - the instrument panel and steering
column are simply transplanted rather than being reversed, leaving the
passenger inspecting the oil-level gauge rather than the clock, and the driver
trying to operate the gear-shift lever and the turn indicators with the same
hand. Interestingly, the air-conditioning control panel is a mirror-image of
the LHD ones, aided by a cunning symmetry in the holes needed for the
switchgear.
	So what's it like to drive? My first reaction is - it's fantastic! The
performance, whether in traffic or on the open road, is everything you'd 
expect, and it certainly isn't either finicky or prone to spinning wheels if
you're incautious - at least in the dry.
	And the handling? Well, I've been driving moderately-powered 
front-wheel-drive cars for the last 14 years. My instincts are pre-programmed 
to an action of "plant the foot, steer out of trouble, and then analyze what's 
going on" when a cornering crisis occurs. With a car as powerful as the 911, 
I'm sure this could lead me into big trouble. Yeah, I know, "slow in, fast 
out", but something tells me they don't mean "slam on all the power" when they 
say "fast out". So, this is just a long-winded way of saying that I'm still 
working up to exploring the handling limits. I've noticed that the road tests 
of each year's new 911 model say things like "this year's 911 is the 
best-handling yet; it's virtually dispelled the old oversteer spectre of last
year's model". The trouble is, they've been saying that for many years now. I
don't want to find out the expensive way what next year's magazines are going
to say.
	In last week's message to I also mentioned that I'd just returned from 
a quick trip to Newcastle, just north of Sydney on the east coast of Australia 
and about 1650 km (1000 miles) from Adelaide. I used the fast route to 
Newcastle, via Ouyen and Wagga Wagga, going through one corner of Victoria on 
my way from South Australia to New South Wales. (Refer to your atlas, and 
don't be surprised if you can't find Ouyen - population only 1500, but a 
significant junction in north-western Victoria.) The roads are not really
"outback", but neither are they exactly highways. About three-quarters of the
distance is on two-lane bitumen, some of it badly cut up by recent floods and
hurriedly patched. Near the state capitals (Adelaide and Sydney) there are long
stretches of four-lane divided highway to what we'd call freeway standard -
controlled access, and few or no bends worth slowing down for. The speed limit
on these sections, and on most of the two-lane stuff, is 110 km/h (68 mph), but
nobody seems to bother if you use a little more in SA or NSW. I set the cruise
control to about 125 km/h (80 mph) except in Victoria, which has an open-road
limit of 100 km/h and a "lock 'em up and throw away their licence" attitude for
30 km/h over the limit.
	Once away from the cities, driving on these roads is a new experience.
In South Australia and Victoria, the road wanders back and forth for no obvious 
reason across rolling country, punctuated every 50 km or so by a signpost
announcing that you've arrived at places with names like "Linga" or "Walpeup".
Most of these are nothing more than railway sidings with road access to the 
silos used to hold wheat during the harvesting season. Further on, near Hay in 
New South Wales, it's sheep and cattle country, and you're likely to come
across a drover with horse or motor-bike (and caravan with TV antenna!) moving
a few thousand animals along the road, presumably on his way to the stockyards
where he'll sell them. In a bad season, when drought affects the properties,
some farmers resort to using "the long paddock" - the strip of land beside the
road, taking advantage of the grass growing in the meagre rainfall runoff from
the road surface. In the daytime, you encounter these animals spread across the
road, making it necessary to stop and wait for them to move across. At night,
they're generally held behind temporary fencing, leaving you to worry about the
occasional animal that may have escaped and is chewing its cud in the middle of
the road.
	Night driving in this area has another hazard. Kangaroos come out of
the bush around dusk, and graze along the roadside or snooze in the middle of
the road till late in the evening. Their dark fur makes them hard to see in the
dark, they jump unpredictably when frightened, and they make an awful mess of a
car if you hit them at speed. The trail of roo corpses by the side of the road
showed that they had been active the previous few nights; I chose to spend the
night in a motel rather than risk bending some expensive bodywork. If a mere
raccoon can do the engine damage reported in an earlier message, I reckon a
fully-grown roo could take out a lot of bodywork on the way through to the
engine. 
	Incidentally, risk of such surprises and the low traffic density leads
to an interesting adaptation of high-beam etiquette. You spend most of your
time with high beam on, and many cars (and all the semis) have a pair of 100W
or 130W driving lights as well. Dropping to low beam when you first see an
oncoming vehicle leaves you almost blind on an unlit and poorly-marked road,
and you may have to drive another 2-3 km before you actually meet him. What
the semi drivers and more experienced night travellers do is to leave the
lights on high beam until the other driver's lights bother you. As soon as one 
driver dips, so does the other. This means you both get the benefit of high
beam for as long as possible. If things go wrong (for example, one driver was 
on low beam when you first see each other), a quick flash gets you back in 
step. 
	Continuing our trip, two-thirds of the way across, around Narrandera,
you encounter the start of the Great Dividing Range, the mountain range that
runs down the east coast of Australia. Here it is merely hilly country, but it
does mean that you encounter traffic, mainly semis, straining to get up steep
climbs. On the two-lane roads, this sometime means waiting for an opportunity
to pass. Such opportunities are a lot easier to find in the 911 than I'm used
to!
	Soon we join the Hume Highway from Melbourne to Sydney, which carries 
a lot more traffic and has had a lot more money spent on it. It's mostly 
four-lane divided road, very smooth, and suitable for much higher speeds.
Unfortunately, it carries a lot more traffic, so it's wise to stick with the 
traffic flow and "dawdle" into Sydney at around 120 km/h (75 mph). Once in 
Sydney, we average about 40 km/h (25 mph) through the suburbs until the start
of the Newcastle Expressway, which is to my eyes a stunning piece of 
engineering - some huge excavations to get a fast 4- and 6-lane highway 
through mountainous country. 
	And so we arrive in Newcastle after 16 hours of driving time, an 
average speed of 102 km/h (64 mph) with a fuel consumption of 9.7 litres/100km.
(Sorry, I haven't got a conversion factor for US gallons, but I can tell you
it's 29 mpg Imperial.) Fuel was a problem; there's a stretch of 700 km (440
miles) where we couldn't find hi-octane unleaded, but with careful planning
there's no need to resort to ordinary unleaded. The fuel gauge didn't help
though - several times it dropped to empty after less than a tenth of a
tankful, only to recover after a few hundred kilometres, leaving the fuel
warning light on and the needle pointing to full!
	A recent test of the 911 by an Australian car magazine claimed it was
"hyper-impractical" for Australian conditions - bumpy, uncomfortable, and so
on. I can only report that it was no more uncomfortable than my 1987
Civic; a stiff suspension, certainly, but the larger wheels seemed to give a 
better ride through potholes. On the trip I found the performance, well, more 
than adequate. My usual passing technique of "select the right gear, keep the 
foot flat until you're ahead of the passee, then smoothly return to your own 
lane" had to be modified somewhat - it's all too easy to arrive at 160 km/h 
(100 mph) before starting the "smooth return" phase. 
	I'm more than happy with my $50 Cabrio; it seems petty to complain 
about the number of faults it had when delivered. But after owning four 
Hondas, I was surprised to find so many things wrong with this "quality" car 
when it was delivered: cruise control not working, two-inch long threads
hanging from the stitching in the roof, trim bolts falling out in the boot, 
the rear lights and red "Porsche" panel showing widespread crazing and 
condensation inside, ignition interference in the cassette player, and the 
fuel gauge problems. The local Porsche dealer has fixed or is fixing the 
problems I've reported so far, so I guess I haven't much cause for complaint. 
	My only remaining problem is that I can't really justify driving 
around in a car worth more than my house. (Or so my wife tells me.) When the 
used Porsche market has followed the increase in prices of new models, we'll 
probably be selling this one and buying a house and a more reasonably-priced 
car. Who knows - it may even be a Porsche!

Vas -

If the glass is undamaged, there is no need I can see for replacing it.  It
probably needs to be disassembled, cleaned, and reassembled with fresh adhesive.
This is apparently quite a bit of work.  Jim Selders had his rear glass replaced
not too long ago; ask him what the parts and labour were.

I have been told by a knowledgeable friend of one common source of squeaks and
rattles from the rear.  There is a less than ideal mating between the glass and
the top fixture/trim.  He found one temporary cure was to wedge thin pieces of
cardboard between the glass and the trim around the top edge.  The cardboard he
used was the wrapper that single-edge razor blades come in.  This worked fine
for a while, until the pressure of the wedged cardboard pushed the fixture
further apart, and the rattle returned.  This after PCNA replaced the hatch once
in attempt to cure the rattle.  Both he and PCNA have given up!

Another source of rattling is the cone-headed bolts which lock the hatch down. 
They can be adjusted with a 13 mm. wrench.  You can judge which side is rattling
by pushing down on the spoiler with the hatch closed, looking for audible travel
(so to speak).  Loosen one or both bolts, and depending on whether the hatch is
currently rattling or sticking, turn it a quarter turn at a try in the
appropriate direction, then retighten.  For those of us who drive our 944's all
year, this is a seasonal adjustment which must be made to compensate for average
ambient temperature!

There is after-market glass available for some if not all Porsche applications. 
One friend has considered using US-made glass for his 930's front window because
of its hardness.  German glass, we are told, is softer and therefore more prone
to chipping and pitting.  Anyone know whether 944 glass can be had?

Kevin Gross

Subject: CAR COVERS!!!



Just a short note to reiterate my third and most valuable deterrent in
car destruction...

To put it clearly, I love my car cover.  It makes me feel so good to know
that not only will the burglar find it an extra step towards getting in
but, it hides all the contents, protects it from the sun, makes my shiny red
car a grey blob, makes it difficult to ticket (for those with parking permits
because the officers don't like to look under it, at least at my school),
keeps it clean and shiny for weeks longer, makes parking easier on the
conscience (door dings chipping the paint just don't happen), reduces
the probability of keying, pisses off tow-trucks (which doubles as another
really nasty way to steal cars), AND can serve as a blanket if it gets 

cold one morning waiting for the ski-lifts to open and you're freezing...

It's definitely worth the investiment.  It takes me less than a minute to
put it on or take it off.  And even a junky one is better than none (quite
unlike the running parts :-)

So there's my yen on the subject...

James

Subject: Preventing break-in, insurance coverage

>>  >Vas, I just had a rear glass installed after a break-in.  
>>  
>>  Sooooo, this is how people muck with Porsches is it?  
>>  What happened exactly?
>>  Did somebody just try to jump in right through your car cover?

>>  thievery or maliciousness.  Whaddaya think about the crime rates 
>>  of our cars? 

	James (and porschephiles) -

	Breaking in thru the huge expanse of rear hatch glass is
a new one on me.  My Porsche (944, followed by 944S) has been broken 
into 3X over the past 5 years.  Each time it was thru the driver's side
glass - twice by bashing the driver's side back seat window glass, once 
by bashing in the entire driver's side (front seat) window glass.
Each break-in was for different reasons: CDs were stolen (hope they 
like my taste in tunes!), once the car was simply searched for goodies. 

	Here's what I've done to prevent theft of *contents* (break-in):

	1) I have since installed a *removable* stereo
	   ... since the last break-in provided me with an opportunity to, 
	   ahem, reconsider my choice of stereos.  I have had a nice CD/stereo 
	   player, and since the head unit was stolen the last time my car was 
	   in for repair (!), I thought it wise to remove a visible 
	   enticement to break in.  Likewise,

	2) I keep my CDs (and valuables!) out of sight
	   in a case *in the trunk* (in one of the two "saddle bags") 
	   when I'm parked.  I used to keep CDs in a black nylon case 
	   behind the front seat - that I thought matched my black interior 
	   well enough to be unobtrusive. But, last January, someone relieve 
	   me of them on a snowy night (along with my window glass), and I 
	   spent a long, cold drive reconsidering that decision.

	3) I carefullly select my parking place.
	   Try to select someplace that is out in the open, to present some
	   risk to a potential thief.  (I don't know how much this helps,
	   as the car has been "properly parked" every time the car has been 
	   looted.  Perhaps this has deterred some additional other thieves, 
	   but I don't know).

	4) factory alarm 
	   This has helped to frighten away at least one thief, and prevent 
	   theft of the stereo or additional items in the car.
	   I have considered additional devices, like a fuel cut-off 
	   to prevent theft of the car itself.


	NOTE: Do not *avoid* making claims for theft of vandalism to your 
	insurance agent for fear of increasing your premium.  Your auto 
	insurance will cover all acts of vandalism or theft under your 
	Comprehensive coverage (minus your Comp deductible), and will not 
	affect your premium (at least for my coverage with State Farm; 
	check your agent or policy).  My agent called this a "non-chargeable 
	claim."


	Any other ideas?

		Robert Duncan
		AT&T Bell Labs, Naperville, IL

Robert Duncan wrote:

>	3) I carefullly select my parking place.
>	   Try to select someplace that is out in the open, to present some
>	   risk to a potential thief.  

	"Out in the open" can be a mixed blessing! When in Sydney (in a lesser 
vehicle) last March, I chose to park my car in an open well-lit car park 
visible from my room, rather than in the dark basement car park. Late the 
next afternoon, a hailstorm struck that area of Sydney. Newspaper headlines 
described the hailstones as "the size of tennis balls", and had photos to back 
it up. My car suffered over $A4000 damage. The one fun bit was filling out the
box on the insurance claim form that said "How many other vehicles were
involved in the incident?". I wrote "30000, according to newspaper reports".

	Still, this is the only time I've had any sort of damage (beyond
parking lot paint chips) to a parked car, and I've been driving for 25 years.
I've also never been broken into. I guess there are some advantages to living 
out here where the more extreme forms of "civilization" haven't spread yet.

I bought a bleed tool also, but it was horrible.  I threw it away, finally.  I

I don't know about your brakes specifically, but 2 bleed valves means, yes, you

bottle.  You really need two people.

Original-From: homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 201 949 6348)
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: brake bleeding

	From: Robert Merithew <jessica.stanford.edu!merithew>
	Subject: recommend a brake bleed tool?
	
	Can someone recommend a *specific* tool and/or method for bleeding
	the brakes on my '74 914 2.0?  The Haynes says there is only one
	bleeder nipple on each rear caliper.  Not true.  Both have two.  Do
	I need to bleed from both nipples on all wheels?  My 'One-man brake
	bleed tool' really sucks - the hose pops off the nipple before the
	one-way valve lets brake fluid out... If I hold the hose on the
	nipple, part of the tool falls apart...
	
	
I use a simple homemade pressure bleeding system. Find a spare brake
reservoir cap at a swap meet (or buy one), drill a hole in the top,
and insert a tire valve and stem. I used the type for mag wheels which
is metal, has rubber gaskets, and srcews on. The technique is to replace
the regular cap with this one while bleeding, and apply about 20 PSI of
air pressure to the tire valve. I use an air chuck which clips onto the
tire valve (gas stations use them for setting the bead when mounting tires).
If you have a compressor, just set the regulator to 20 PSI. You could
also use an air tank, such as the converted Freon bottles. Be careful
not to apply much more than 20 PSI or so, or you can make a real mess.

Once you have the top of the reservoir pressurized, just go to each
wheel, hook up a piece of tubing, and open the bleeder nipple.
Do all of the nipples you can find, but the ones which get most air out
are (not surprisingly) on the top of the caliper. This works very well
for me, on a variety of cars, including 914s. The only concern is that
it is very easy to bleed a lot of fluid through, so you can easily
run the reservoir dry (which means you have to start over). Check the
reservoir often. Good luck.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

I did read something about using unleaded gas in older Porsches.  I think Bruce
Anderson wrote it.  If I remember correctly, the real risk is to the valves
and/or their seats.  Apparently Porsche valves/seats have always been
constructed of a hard enough materials that unlead fuels are OK.  With the
advent of unleaded fuels, other manufacturers started using similar materials.

I might expect that your car's time would require some adjustment with a lower
octane fuel.  So you might be trading off a bit of performance against holed
pistons.  There must be someone out there who can speak more convincingly to
this point!

Kevin Gross

Patty,

A number of things come into play when I'm deciding whether to put the
car cover on or not:  Time of day, length of stay, weather conditions,
location.

If the sun is shining, I like to put it on no matter what.  If I'm 

planning on staying longer than an hour, I like to put it on.
If I'm in an unfamiliar place but don't fear for my life then
I'll put it on (if it's too dangerous, it's better to drive off
in a keyed car then get mugged while trying to uncover your unkeyed
car.)

It's just a matter of getting in a routine of doing it a little more often
than you're inclined to do it.  Forgive the analogy but... it's sort
of like prophylactics. :-\ 


James

Subject: Re: unleaded gas

> I did read something about using unleaded gas in older Porsches.  I think Bruce
> Anderson wrote it.  If I remember correctly, the real risk is to the valves
> and/or their seats.  Apparently Porsche valves/seats have always been
> constructed of a hard enough materials that unlead fuels are OK.  With the
> advent of unleaded fuels, other manufacturers started using similar materials.
> I might expect that your car's time would require some adjustment with a lower
> octane fuel.  So you might be trading off a bit of performance against holed
> pistons.  There must be someone out there who can speak more convincingly to
> this point!

If you are talking about the air-cooled engines, I think I can
comment with some confidence:

The heat transfer picture in the engine depends on a number of factors,
amoung which are the enthalpy of vaporization of the fuel and the flame-
front propagation rate, as well as the fuel combustion efficiency.

These characteristics of modern unleaded fuels can be quite different from
the equivalent characteristics of the high-test leaded fuels your engine
was designed to run on if it was built before about 1973 (I think). A
VW/Porsche mechanic told me that they get about a degree (C) of engine
temperature decrease per octane point increase, and recommended
that high octane fuel should be used to prevent thermally induced stress
from cracking the block at the studs.


-- 
---
John R. Lupien
lupienj@hpwarq.hp.com

Subject: Re:  recommend a brake bleed tool?

Imparts sells a brake bleeding tool which is quite excellent.
I have used mine a lot and it works nicely.
It creates a vacuum and collects the fluid of each caliper in a
little plastic container. The tool is fairly inexpensive, I think
$39 or so, and works really good. Snap on sells a very similar one.
It costs a lot more so. It has however one advantage. The imparts tool
requires that you have pretty big hands, as the pump levers are big
and spread apart pretty far. The snap-on tool is much handier, yet much
more expensive and they both do the same job. The tool kit from imparts
includes a vacuum pump complete with gauge, the brake fluid collecting
container, hoses and tons of different adapters for different sizes and
kinds of bleed valves. It further comes with a nice case and a nice
manual for vacuum trouble shooting.

Frank

Robert Merithew asks where one of the two vacuum hoses from the distributor

To: merithew@jessica.stanford.edu, porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: re:914 dist. vacuum hose goes nowhere...

Robert Merithew asks:
>I noticed that one hose from my vacuum advance/retard unit goes nowhere.
There is one hose that follows a circuitous route to the throttle body.
Where (physically & logically) is the other (smaller) hose supposed to
go?  It is a '74 914 2.0...

thanks,
  -Robert Merithew

Logically is easier (it has been about 17 yrs since I worked on these): the
connection to the throttle body should be the advance hose.  I believe that
it responds to vacuum just prior to the throttle plate (if the engine is
under load, and a large volume of air is passing through the venturi, then
vacuum would be relatively high at this location.  If the car is idling or
decelerating, then vacuum would be relatively low at this location).

The other connection is probably direct to the intake manifold or intake
air distributor.  It is located after the throttle body, and is intended
to provide a source of vacuum which is high when the car is decelerating.
The vacuum retard line should be connected to it.

If the vacuum retard is not connected, then you have a vacuum leak which
contributes to poor idling performance.

914s are very sensitive to vacuum leaks.  There are a number of steps you
can take to prevent problems, however.  Here are some things you might want
to look for:

  1.  make sure that all vacuum hoses are in good condition and connected.  Look
      at the ends of the hoses for any small cracks in the rubber.  Sometimes
      cutting off 1/2" solves the problem, but it is a good idea to get some
      spare vacuum advance hose.

  2.  make sure that the crankcase is tight: the O-ring in the oil filler should
      be in good condition, the valve cover gaskets should be installed properly,
      the pcv hoses should be in good condition.

  3.  check the distributor operation: remove the cap, rotor, and dust cover,
      and apply vacuum to the advance and retard lines to the distributor, one
      at a time (I use my mouth, but there are hand operated vacuum pumps
      that you can get for this).  Watch the plate that the points are on,
      and make sure that the advance and retard are working.  It is very
      common for the diaphragms to start leaking, which provides a vacuum 
      leak and prevents proper operation of the distributor.  If the advance
      works, but it sticks or seems difficult, dissassemble the distributor
      and reassemble after cleaning and adding some silicone grease to the 
      bearing surfaces.

  4.  check the hoses between the intake manifolds and air distributor.  There
      are four of these, about 2 inches long and about  1.5 inches in diameter.

Another problem area is the injector seals.  Each injector has a seal at the
tip (a rubber donut about 1/2 inch in diameter).  You have to pull the injectors
to inspect them, but if you haven't replaced your fuel hose recently, you can
head off some future maintenance (and prevent the possibility of an engine fire)
by doing both operations at the same time.
	
-Dave Erickson         erickson@cs.nps.navy.mil

4 cylinder Porsche engine. No manifolds or carbs. All position related
commentary is relative to a driver sitting in the driver's seat.

Oil cooler top left, mounted between the two cylinders. Oil pressure

middle right. Fuel pump looks a lot like a Bug pump, but with a plastic
dome over the top.

3 ball T throttle linkage, horizontal mounting on mast forward of fuel pump.

Hoo boy......the voice crying in the wilderness...saying "How can I make 
my car smog legal and still have it be worth driving?" 

The simple answer is, you can't have it be smog legal and get decent
performance. 

Now, this is not to say that you can't have decent performance at the 
same emission levels, but you can't be *legal*. 

The reason is that that law is written so that you must only use absolute
stock parts to be legal. I believe that this was designed to take older
cars off the road as parts become harder to find and put you in a new
Ford Escort or something....

So nothing but old Zeniths or Solexes are legal. Nothing but the Bosch
Vacuum Advance unit that came stock is legal, no aftermarket anything
anywhere is legal. 

So you get to do what thousands of other people do.....which is to keep
all the smog stuff around and put it on at smog time. 

Or you can make an appointment at a "referee" station and plead that the
older stuff is all worn out and replacements are either No Longer Available
or prohibitively expensive. ($300 will do it...) Then the referee makes sure 
that your emissions are not too egregious and gives you a sticker saying that 
motor number ###########-####### is exempt from that portion of the law that
says you gotta be bone stock .

It's a screwed law. It should be that if you pass, you pass, and if you
don't pass, *then* they check to see if you're stock.....but that's not
how the legislature works.....recto-cranial inversion.....

JZ
 	    If you're ever going to stand on it, stand on it now.
		Because the older you get, the slower you go.

To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Re:  legal smog 912 equipment

> Hoo boy......the voice crying in the wilderness...saying "How can I make 
> my car smog legal and still have it be worth driving?" 

> ...  you can make an appointment at a "referee" station and plead that the
> older stuff is all worn out and replacements are either No Longer Available
> or prohibitively expensive. ($300 will do it...) 

This is what I thought, too, but when I was looking for a used car to
buy I looked into it.  I ended up phoning the ARB, who said that there is
no dollar limit which allows you to replace required smog equipment with
non-approved smog equipment.  Further, there are no approved replacements 
for Solexes.  The only way around it would be if the Solexes were no longer 
available.  Since they are still available (at a cost of $1000/pair?), Webers 
are not legal.

I asked "what if the Solexes are worn out?". Then, for a '69 car, if
you have already spent $50 trying to get the car smogged, you
can go to a referee station, who can give you permission to use the
worn out Solexes, but cannot give you permission to use new Webers!

The $300 limit comes into play for later model cars.  Apparently the
referee station can require owners of such cars to purchase new smog
equipment to replace worn equipment, if the cost does not exceed $300.

-Dave Erickson

Actually, the booklet I got from the DMV says that '65 model year foreign
cars have to be smogged when they change owners and never otherwise...but
that '66 and later have to be smogged eveyr two years and when they 
change owners. 

Just for the amusement of the group, here is a segment of a book I'm working
on about living with old VWs in the 90s. Comments are appreciated, of course...


* The Wonderful Intelligent California Smog Law:


Since 90% of the crummy Volkswagens left alive in the world live in California,
we have to deal with the wonderful California Smog Law. So you probably 
want to know what the smog law is, right? Well, it's like this, it doesn't 
matter what the law is because each individual smog station is a little
law unto itself. Technically, your car must have all the engine parts 
just as it came from the factory. 

The amount and complexity of smog gear goes up exponentially as your car 
gets newer. By 1974, there are exhaust gas recirculation systems, vacuum
retard as well as advance on the distributor, and a host of other bizarre
little devices. Add this to the fact that most mechanics in your neighborhood
garage are not experts on the various changes that have gone on in the 
smog gear of every car on the road down through the years and you have the
makings for a pretty chaotic smog inspection system. A quick note, 1974
California cars have a different muffler and intake manifold ......if you
have a 1974, be prepared to pay up to $100 for a new muffler. Ouch! 

But the clincher on smog is this. No matter what you do or what is connected
to what, the inspection still consists of a guy looking at your car 
and deciding if everything is hooked up correctly and then checking 
your car's emissions with a little machine. The Law prevents them from
hooking up the little machine and checking to see if your car passes
the test if they don't think everything is hooked up correctly. I have
taken totally legal VWs to the various smog stations and been assured
by an "expert mechanic" that I needed to hook up the carb warm air intake
into the heater box to make it legal. This is blatantly absolutely 
postively wrong and I told the guy so and showed him why, but he didn't
believe me. I changed it, because I needed a certificate and he wasn't
going to give it to me until I did it his way. Every smog dude is a petty
tyrant, and gives or witholds those precious little certificates as they 
please.

I've also taken cars in with tubes randomly plugged on to every  tube 
mounting point, had the guys give it a look and then they merrily wrote
the certificate. The bottom line is this: Volksies almost always can pass
the smog check if they are reasonably stock. The big killers are
high-performance camshafts and super-huge carburetors. But there is no
difference between a mechanical advance distributor and a vacuum assist
distributor at the smog check, except that they won't write you a certificate
with one and they will write it with the other. 

So get your car smog legal as best as you can and get down to your local
station. If they say you have to run a tube from your windshield washer 
bottle to your right front headlight to pass, go ahead and do it. These 
guys are the ones who sign the certificates and that's really all that
matters. If things really don't seem quite right with what they want, 
try a different one....that's the beauty of this confused situation, since
every one does it their own way, sooner or later, you'll find one that 
does it *your* way. (provided your way is reasonable.)

There is a cut off point that you will want to know about if you intend
to modify your car "for off road use only" with parts that "may never
be used on a pollution controlled vehicle." (in my estimation, about 75%
of VWs on the road are not smog legal.) California currently requires
that smog checks be performed every other year on cars of model year
1966 and newer. I've gotten several different stories from the DMV
(where each clerk also does things their own way) but I think the
prevailing view is that 1965 cars have to get a smog certificate 
when they change owners but only then, and 1964 and older cars never
need smog certificates. The rules are different for American cars,
but this is about Volkswagens, right? 

So if you decide to make your car go faster than the factory intended
and faster than the department of motor vehicles wants you to go, you
have some choices to make. If your car is a '64 or older, do whatever
you want! If your car is a 1965, you can get it smogged once and then
go to town on the improvements, but if you have a 1966 or later car,
you are going to have to get it smogged every other year until
we elect politicians with brains. (and we all know when that will be...) 
You can make it so that with a few hours of work, you can replace the carbs
and distributor and all the tubes and wires with the stock stuff and go 
get your certificate, or you can keep a spare engine around that's all
smogged up and ready to go. (this is a little more difficult, and really
unnecessary unless your real motor has a high-performance cam and other
goodies under the cooling tin. 

One more note about smog. You are allowed to upgrade to later equipment. 
The way it works is thus, if your car is a 1970, you must have 1970 smog 
gear on it. If you have *more* smog gear, you're OK, you just can't have 
*less* smog gear. So if you have bought a car and had three separate smog
guys charge you $40 a pop to tell you that "ain't no way this baby's gonna
pass unless you let me put a new-rebuilt $700 motor in it" you can start
cruising the parts sections of the advertiser for someone parting out a 
similar volksie to yours and go buy the complete, hopefully smog legal
motor for $75 or so. It may not run well, but it's better than letting
the service station overcharge you and keep your nifty go-fast motor
(as a "core") while he puts *his* $75 cruddy motor in your car.....A central
truth....the smog guys *always* get smog certificates when they want, because
they write them!

If the whole smog thing is a real tragedy, you may have to take the car to a 
mechanic and get it put into reasonable shape. If you have to do this,
make sure you ask for all your old parts back! I guarantee you your car
will not run well after being smogged. You may find you want to sell these
parts for something approaching market value, or put them back on. 

Conversely, if you have a smog legal car and are planning to modify it, don't
throw those stock parts away! They are worth their weight in new go-fast
parts because you, me and every other volksie owner needs them to get those
damned smog certificates! So sell them at the swap meet, keep them in your
garage, or mail them to me, but don't let them go to the dump! The best
route is to keep them in a box, because when you sell the car, you are legally
responsible for ensuring that it is smog legal. So save the future owner a
world of hassle and hang on to those parts! 


+++++++++++++++

JZ

The '3' in the spark plug number you have indicates heat range.  The heat range
depends on exactly what kind of '73 911 you have.  According to Bruce Anderson's
'Porsche 911 Performance Handbook,' it breaks down as follows for the '73 cars:

        911T W235P21/W5DP, gapped to .6mm
        911E W265P21/W3DP2 gapped to .55 mm
        911S W265P21/W3DP2 gapped to .55 mm

Performance Products carries them.  If they haven't been changed or gapped in
five years, you're long overdue for a fresh set!

Hopes this helps.

Kevin Gross

From: bowers@elxsi.dfrf.nasa.gov
Subject: coils vs. torsion bars and 4v heads...
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu

Mark Skala asks:
 
>Question for the enlightened:  I have first-hand experience with torsion
>bars problems with 914's.  Torsion bars must be protected by having them
>painted, coated with grease, and with good bushings, or a little scratch
>will cause the bars to rust and break within days.  The question is, how
>is it that coil springs can be mounted relatively out in the open, where
>rocks and salt and other hazards can get at them, but they don't seem to
>break the same way?  Don't coils use similar principles as torsion bars?
 
Coils and torsion bars operate on the same priciple, but in effect operate on
the steel in different ways.  This is based on geometry and the principle axis
that is being strained.  Strain is the amount of deformation that one puts
into the steel, usually expressed as inches per inch.  This is a measured
parameter and is usually intsrumented with strain gages.  The implied part of
the equation (to find the force applied) is buried in the geometry of the part
you are measuring the strain in.  To calibrate it is done either by measuring
the part, and calculating the strain for a given load (from stress) or by
emperically loading the part and measuring the strain directly (a good
experimentalist will do both, just for a reality check).  But back to coils
and torsion bars.
 
Coils work on bending the steel from one shape to another, there is some
twisting but it is inconsequential compared to the bending of the unstressed
shape.  A torsion bar works by twisting.  This implies that the maximum strain
occurs at the surface and the minimum strain occurs in the center of the area
moment of inertia (another topic).  Bending causes a strain at the outside
edges as well. but here the area that is under maximum strain is very small in
area, a compressive area and a tension area.  As both these areas are small
and move in relation to the amount of compression the coil is under it is much
more difficult to damage a coil with a ding or scratch than it is with a
torsion bar.  There is another mitigating reason as well.  The strain for a
given load in a coil with the correct spring constant (k in engineering) is
much lower than the the same load with the correct `k' in a torsion bar.  This
means the stress at the surface of the torsion bar is inherently higher.
 
Why torsion bars instead of coils in the first place?  Volume.  A torsion bar
isn't very big, volume wise, compared to a coil to do the same job.
 
As for the question about the water cooled 4 valve heads, they were first used
on the 935/78.  And there are lots of these heads around, but they aren't
cheap (relatively speaking).
 
just my opinion...
 
Al Bowers

Well, some spring principals are the same and some are different.  Coil springs
have some torsional load on them, but it's not as great as an axial torsion
spring [any physics types care to elaborate?].

and is it hollow or solid?  Sounds like low grade steel to me.  I've had
Weltmeister bars on for 3+ years and there's nothing but dirt on them.  They
don't rust.  At least you get good quality materials for the bucks they charge!

Subject: wrong throttle body on my 914 ?
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Date: Fri, 26 Oct 90 1:21:25 PDT
X-Mailer: ELM [version 2.3 PL0]

1974 porsche 914 2.0l (orig. sold in CA)

I recently asked where the two vaccuum hoses from my distributor
advance/retard unit were supposed to connect.  A few people said that
a medium-sized hose goes under the distributor and around to the back
of the throttle body (near the idle adjust screw) and that a smaller
hose goes directly from the distributor to the front of the throttle
body.  One person said the small hose is supposed to go nowhere.  A
dealer seemed to think that both hoses go to the throttle body as
above.

Robert Merithew asks:
    Could someone with a 2.0l 914 look at the part number on their
    throttle body 

     039 133 067

Well, as some of you know, I've been parting out my 2.0 914, so I looked
at my part #, and it's exactly the same as the one Robert quoted.
This one has TWO hose connections.  Then I dug through some of my fuel
system stuff I got at a swap meet, and found my other throttle body in
there, and it also has this exact same part #, but only ONE hose connection.
When you put them side by side, you can tell the castings are the same,
and where the hose connections are there is a sort of raised circular
plateau where they can either drill a hole and weld these connections
or not.  The one with only one connection is either from a 74 2.0 engine
or a whatever year 1.7l engine, which are the cars that I got the pieces
from (in a box).  My advice to you, since these throttle bodies aren't
exactly wear items that you can get easily, is to drill the other hole
yourself, if you can find a metal tube to accept the hose.

Mark A. Skala             Porsche: The best defense is a good offense.

Bruce,

I've generally solved interior plastic-to-plastic squeaks with silicone spray
lube, this in a 944-series car.  The biggest offender has been the sun roof's
pop-up wind deflector, where it inserts into the removeable sun roof.  Other
folks have solved similar squeaks where the A-pillar's interior meets the dash.

Silicone seems to last reasonably long.  Since it's not greasy, it doesn't catch
and hold bits of grit (like a grease would).  Nor does it bleed or wick out.  I
reapply it whenever I have the sunroof out for cleaning, which turns out to be
about once a year.  I "mask" the spraying with a couple of drop cloths; no
reason to get silicon all over the interior.

Hope this helps.  I would be interested in hearing what you've tried.  Where
exactly is the squeak coming from?

Kevin Gross

Date: Mon, 29 Oct 90 08:31:24 est
From: Jeffrey A. Shorey <js@dukee.egr.duke.edu>
Message-Id: <9010291331.AA08285@dukee.egr.duke.edu>
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: 911 Timing Cover Gaskets

   I replaced the timing cover gaskets  on my 911 this weekend, and thought
I would relate some of my experiences to anyone else wanting to do the same
thing to their car.

  The reason I decided to replace them in the first place was because I
previously had had a hole in my exhaust which blew directly onto the base
of the right timing cover and evidently toasted the gasket.  Every time the
car got hot and was parked it would drip a little puddle of oil onto the
floor, which stopped after the engine had cooled down sufficiently.  This oil
blew onto the heat exchanger when driving, resulting in the standard 914
"cooking-up-ribs-in-the-back" syndrome.

   To get the the blasted thing, you have to remove the back license plate
panel, the exhaust, the sheet metal on the rear of the engine, and your
AC mounting bracket if you have one.

   I had purchased a timing cover kit which included the lock nuts, gaskets,
and washers.  I got it for around $8, half the price of Tweeks or anybody else
at one of the local Porsche shops in Durham.

   Here's the kicker of the article: you must scrap every little piece of old
gasket off of the chain box and timing cover.  I left a piece that was almost
imperceptible to the fingertips around one of the lower studs.  Evidently a
previous mechanic had injected orange RTV into just the bottom portion of the
gasket to try to get it to stop leaking, causing the gasket to stick and
requiring it to be scraped off.  Leaving this fragment on the chain box caused
oil to _stream_ out of the box when the engine was turned on, no matter
how tight the nuts were tightened.  Overtightening the nuts was another bad
idea - the soft washers flattened out and essentially stuffed themselves down
the holes for the studs, making it a bitch to get the cover back off and
making it necessary to pick the remains off the stud once the cover was
removed.

   So, after learning all of these little tidbits, I got the cover back on
and now the engine leaks not a single bit of oil.  Quite a change from the
914 where no matter what you do, you still have a rolling Valdez.

- Jeff Shorey

Subject: Re: wander...

> In driving over an "open deck bridge" - it is a steel deck that is open
> through, my 944 seems to wander.  Any ideas of what may be the problem or 
> is it no problem at all, just the issue of the tires and suspension on this
> type of road surface?

Probably no problem. Do you have snows on it? (or all weather radials)
They seem to exhibit this behavior more strongly than the "pseudo-slicks"
(A008, A008R) that I run in the summer. Actually anything with real
tread will "grab a line" here and there, and the lines run every which
way on that kind of surface. Another "complication" about such bridges
is the when wet they offer close to zero traction. Wheel-spin at 1/4
throttle in third.

If you are worried about your alignment, though, get it checked.

Joe, your 944 wandering on a mesh-screen bridge deck is normal.  All those
little ridges nibble at your wide front tires and make it track terribly.  The
problem is exacerbated if your front toe is 0 or toe-out; it should be slightly
toe-in.

                JBS

Subject: Re: Targa top
Message-Id: <02901029164720/0003844665NB2EM@mcimail.com>

Well, there are two ways your Targa top can squeak.  First, two surfaces can
move relative to one another at an interface, and second, the top itself can be
stressed and emit noise.

I'd expect that your problem is the former.  If so, it's a matter of locating
all interface points (i.e., any place one part touches another) and lubricating
it.  This may only work for as long as the lubricant wants to stick around, then
your noise will return.

Have someone drive your car while you move around locating the sound source. 
Then stop and examine things to find the nasty spot!

Good luck!

                JBS

Cc: porschephiles <porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu>
Subject: re: 914 Throttle body
Message-Id: <85901029164758/0003844665NB2EM@mcimail.com>

Sometimes Porsche revises a part and appends a "rev number" to it.  There should
be a two-digit rev after the number to indicate this.  Your cast intake parts
may not show it, but if you go to a dealer and look at the book, it'll probably
be an -01 or greater number.

Sometimes the revision is just to improve a part, and it remains compatible with
the old part.  In other cases, the rev'ed part is entirely different and cannot
be retrofitted.

For example, my 944 hood struts got rev'ed once and it entailed coloring the
strut rods black and improving the bottom mount point slightly - totally
compatible.  But the front brake rotors also have a -01 rev number, but that's
for the negative offset front end; the -00 and -01 rotors are entirely different
things!  I guess this means that if they ever change the -00 rotors, they have a
problem with the part numbers...

                JBS

I'm posting this long musing to the whole list because I think it
will have fairly general interest.  The Porschephiles list is not that
active so I think bandwidth is available.  If someone wants to scold
me for excessive posting - go ahead, I can take it.

Ah let's see, where to begin...

I decided that I wanted 1976 or later because of the new body style and
zinc coating to reduce rust.  I have read and heard alot about problems
with '76 and '77 models.  Perhaps more so in CA versions.  Five bladed
cooling fan, magnesium engine cases, thermal reactor system all have a
bad reputation.  Read Bruce Andersons 911 Performance handbook.  The
3.0 engine which came on line in '78 is really the way to go.  He raves
about their strength and longevity.  I would have taken any good car in
years '78 to '8whatever based upon cost.  I didn't want red - I
definitely wanted a Targa.  I live in San Diego so I was reading all
San Diego sources of information and the Los Angeles Times as well.  On
any given weekend there were probably three or fewer cars that came
close to the above criteria.

I looked for about three months, sometimes I think perhaps I should
have looked longer but I wanted the car and I was using alot of time
looking, test driving, and daydreaming.  I looked at probably ten cars
in the area including LA and Orange County and probably rejected three
times that many over the phone.  I finally paid $18K for the car ($1.5K
off asking price).  That is a decent but not particularly good price.
It would have been better had the car been in the condition that I have
since brought it to.  To give an idea of what I have done to get it up to
what I begin to consider Prosche standards since I've had it I've (note
that I'm kind of a fanatic):

1.  Had the Targa top interior redone ($85.00).

2.  Replaced scads of minor interior, exterior and engine pieces
including getting proper plug wires ($300-500).

3.  Added new style hydraulic chain tensioners (1980 already has the
new larger chain idler wheels - a plus) and did alot of clean up
rerouting, cable tying, hose replacing in the engine compartment.
($350.00 or so).

4.  Replaced brake lines and leaking master cylinder ($150.00).

5.  Replaced Targa bar seal ($130.00).

6.  Front spoiler repainted with ding resistant paint ($200.00).

7.  Replaced non-functional and funky alarm system ($250.00).

8.  Replaced barely functional and funky stereo system (obviously
discretionary) ($600.00).

9.  Replaced all interior carpeting and door panels (consequence of
earlier stereo installation), rebuilt and recovered door pockets,
miscelaneous interior work ($500.00).

10.  Tune-up, valve adjust and replace all fluids ($100.00).

11.  New voltage regulator.

12.  Found and replaced missing jack, inflator and tool kit (don't
overlook these) ($275.00).

I've probably missed some things; the point is that if the car isn't
perfect, alot of money can go into getting it there.  Note that of
course, this includes parts only.  There are literally hundreds of
hours of my labor which would have to be paid for if you or whoever
isn't mechanically adept.  Of course, I enjoy working on automobiles
and so this is a plus rather than a minus for me, although even for
a fanatic, it has consumed alot of my time over the past eight months.
In spite of all the above, note that before I did any of it, I jumped
in the car after only an oil change and took a 1000 mile trip up the
coast to San Francisco, drove through driving rain (alot) and started
easily in probably 10 degree temperatures in the Sierra Foothills;
these cars are an order of magnitude better and more reliable than
practically anything on the road (remember this is a 10 year old
vehicle).

Other lessons learned:

1.  Know the market, research the cars, take your time looking.

2.  Take your time looking, research the cars, know the market.

3.  Subscribe to Panorama and consider cars from outside of your local
area; I see what look to be alot of good buys, although flying to the
car, financing across state lines and emission regulations (far more of
a consideration to a Californian) make this difficult.

4.  I bought a car without alot of documentation.  I know cars pretty
well, and it sounded fine and it ran very strong, the compression test
was good.  Even so, I should have probably gotten additional expert advice
although overall I feel I made a decent buy.  Its probably worthwhile
to try and get the "perfect one-owner cream-puff with a receipt for
every light bulb replaced since new" but it is difficult.

5.  Look out for battery area corrosion.  Mine has just a touch and
I've spent alot of time cleaning and fighting it.

6.  All the ususal advice about checking for straightness of body
panels, matching numbers, looking for strange paint mismatches, etc.,
read all the books on buying used cars, be especially careful if the
car has been painted.  (If you can't tell, you probably should have
help looking for your car).  The paint on my car is decent but has minor
flaws and this is one area that is *very* expensive to bring up to
standards.  New paint is also, of course, a sign of potential previous
abuse/damage.

7.  Be *very* careful around dealers, "exotic car specialty resellers",
and semi-professional Porsche restorer/sellers.

8.  Insurance is not as bad as everyone makes out, but it is not an
inconsequential expense, check first.

10.  If you can, establish a good relationship with a local shop/parts
seller even if you do your own work.  Alan Johnson Racing here in S.D.
has been very helpful; if you buy alot of parts ask for and expect a
discount.  The Porsche parts network is wonderful, every tiny screw,
nut and plastic thingamagig is available and usually readily (for a
price).

11.  Drive as many as you can if you're not familiar with 911's.  They
have a feel all their own (wonderful!).  Get to know what the clutch,
transmission, steering, power are like when they're right and when
they're not.

12.  Engine parts are expensive: one mechanic advised me to get a good
engine and compromise on body and interior if necessary, this is what
I guess I basically did (I hope).

I still expect to:

1.  Replace the clutch someday (ref: Bruce Anderson 911 Perf Hdbk).

2.  Perhaps repaint someday (after the payments have stopped).

3.  New trunk carpet.

Don't be put off by all of the above.  Porsches are finely made and in
my limited experience and research very reliable.  Mine is absolutely
a joy to own and drive!  There is no substitute! 

--

	gag.  so why is it the 914 got axed and we still have two-valve

	at least bmw is up front about their obsession with marketing..
	
++++++++++

Probably because Porsche the company is quite different from Porsche the man. 

I think the greatest things come from one person's vision. Many people may lay
hands on a project, but the key to success lies in letting one person have a 
vision and the authority to keep the process on track. 

Many of the great automobiles have been created this way. Ferry Porsche's vision
of the 356 produced great cars. After time, the marketing forces take hold and
you end up with 2 valve 911s for years and years. That's why they killed the
912 and 914.....so they could open up a market for the 924 and then move those
people up to the 944 or 928. 

For another example...

The Datsun 240Z was great. After the marketing forces convinced management that
they could improve the car by "giving people what they wanted" you ended up with
the 280ZX. Barf. 

I think that "marketing" and the general influence of bottom line corporate
types ruins everything it touches.  These are people whose only visions in life
are of big numbers on the bottom lines. They are the ones who make things a
little cheaper to improve the profit margin. They are the ones who say you
have to have a back seat so you can sell to the people with kids. They are
the ones who tell you that convertibles won't sell anymore and then turn 
around and sell the "comeback" of the convertible after they realize they
were wrong. 

Speaking of which, I predict the Mazda Miata will go the way of the 240Z 
and become a Marketing Monstrosity. It will get bigger, more comfy, and 
have more "features." Then they'll introduce the Coupe version......with
a 2+2 rear seat. They'll ruin what is probably a nice car inside of 5 years.

Feh, Ptooey! 

Sorry.....you hit a button. 

JZ

The Miata is nice car.  One of the nicer ones put out in the past few
years.  The first one rolled off the factory line as a Marketing
Monstrosity, though.  We're talking about a car built up, down and
sideways on marketing hype.  It has two seats and is convertible.
End of sports car heritage.

The car feels like a tank compared to an MGB.  Who wants a sports car
with a Cadillac ride?  They spent *weeks* on the exhaust to give it
"the same note as an MGB, only quieter."  And as on any car build in
the last ten years, it's impossible to work on.

The Miata is an example of one of the funner cars being produced
these days.  But it has been marketized and engineered to be
*exactly* what it is: a car that with most features of the sports car
people longed after but none of the spirit.  Jesus H. Fucking Christ,
who needs an air conditioner in a convertible?

Sports car companies: MG, Healey, Porsche, AC, Triumph, Fiat, Alfa,
Datsun (not Nissan), and based on one model only, VW.

Go ahead, flame me.

andy

> Miatas ride like Cadillacs?  ???

I said compared to MGs (and Porsches).  It was a gross comparison.

> Fine, if you feel that a sports car must be uncomfortable, devoid of con-
> venient features, and send your mechanic's kids through college, it's
> your prerogative.  I hardly see how a Miata can be equated to a tank,
> except of course for the fact that it doesn't rattle like an MG.

On my 2 MGs, Porsche, Toyota, and chunky GM Truck, I've given
mechanics enough money in the past three years to send their kids 
to a nice dinner and an evening out on the town. Actually, that was
entirely on the Porsche.

The seats out of the MG are so comfortable that I've put a set in the
truck.  Exactly what convenient features are missing in MGs?

Rattles have been few and far between on the MGs.  Always easily
diagnosed and solved.

In my experience, the Miata has a mushy suspension and lacks in upper
end power, *compared to* the other fun cars in my stable.

I still think it's one of the better cars available new these days.
If you're dead set on getting a *new* fun car, go for it.  It is,
however, a Marketing Monstrosity.  That was the point I was making
to JZ. 

andy

+++++++

So I started taking the EP car apart this weekend. It turns out that the 
only Super 90 part on the car is the bloody Tachometer! All checks on the
motor show it to be a 1962 Normal. 

Now, I have mixed feelings about this. On the one hand, the guy said it
was a Super-90, but I didn't take his word for it and didn't check. (I
could have checked it very easily) Mostly I didn't check because I would
still have wanted it if it was a normal or super. And it's a Normal. 
The big win because it's a normal is that the parts are quite a bit 
less expensive than if it were an S-90. The big loss is about 30 Horsepower. 
But since my wallet is significantly smaller than my desire to be on the
racetrack with it, a normal is probably better for me. Plus normals will
last longer and run better on modern gas. 

The car needs new torsion bars all around, as I discovered that the driver's
side sits about 1.5" lower than the passenger side....it also needs a new
steering box. So we're looking at about $500 right there jsut to make it 
drive around the block. 

It does run pretty well, though. With a carb rebuild, I bet it will run 
Just Fine. 

The big problem of the weekend was one of the &!^#*&^*!&^*)&$^@# rear bumper
bolts. A little 13mm ATF bolt that was rust-welded very tightly. I worked
and strained and pulled and strained and finally went to air-chisel it off,
but surprise surprise surprise, the little bugger ate two of my chisels with
nary a scratch on it. Just goes to show that some grade 8 fasteners are 
more equal than others. I finally clamped a vice grip on the nut and pressed
real hard with a cheater bar and twisted the little f***er's head off. 

The car seems to be responding well. Anyone who tells you that old Porsches
don't know who's touching them and what's going on is a fool. Things are
springing to life that weren't working before. Chitty Chitty Bang Bang 
was not fiction! 

More as it comes....

J(spent all weekend up to my elbows in Pink!)Z

Kept the Porschephiles (since british-cars is still in hiatus);
ignore the hardware, the passion's the same:

>The car seems to be responding well. Anyone who tells you that old Porsches
>don't know who's touching them and what's going on is a fool. Things are
>springing to life that weren't working before. Chitty Chitty Bang Bang 
>was not fiction! 

Yep.  Old MGs are the same way.  We kludged up the choke
(well, cold-start mixture control wire) last weekend, 
in a way that only a Keeper of the Faith would know how
to do (till I get desperate and install the Right Cable,
anyway).  That plus cleaning the dashpots, running a 
little Marvel Mystery Oil down a chopstick (no, I'm not
kidding) and onto the distributor lubrication hole, burnishing
the oxidation off the rotor, wiping out the cap, putting a
little Bosch grease (but not too much!) on the distributor
cam, and topping off the SU dampers with more of Marvel's
finest made a stunning difference.  The cold-start problem
is GONE -- we start with a roar now, and run well enough to
chirp the tires (well, the palaeolithic Dunlops help that)
even before the needle rises up to the C on the temp gauge.

Old sports cars just need to be touched in that special way
every so often.  Just like their owners...  

Scott Fisher, Proprietor
Grizzlies from Hell Race Shop, Brewery, and Child Development Centre,
a Fizzball Racing Technology Partner

Plus if you're really, truly going to EP with that car,

Production ain't Improved Touring, Jeffie.

>From The Book (SCCA Production Category rules), E
Production, Page 11:

Manufacturer: Porsche
Model: 356, 356A, 356C-(1500, 1600), 356C/1600SC, 356B Super 90
       Cabriolet

ENGINE
  Manufacturer         Porsche
  Type                 OHV, 4 cyl. opposed
  Bore & stroke        3.25" x 2.91" or 3.15" x 2.91"
  Capacity             1582cc or 1488cc
  Head material        Alum.
  Block material       Alum.
  Valve head dia.:
    Intake             1.50" or 1.57"
    Exhaust            1.34"
    Induction system   Two Zenith 32 NDIX or Two Solex 32 PBIC or
                       Two Solex 40 PBIC or Two Solex P II 4 or 
                       PJ54

And from the PCS, page 14:

"D.4.  It is permitted to lighten, balance, or modify in shape by 
tooling components of the engine and drivetrain, provided
it is always possible to identify them positively as such.
It is not permitted to add any material or mechanical 
extension unless authorized in these Rules."

Section E, "Engine -- reciprocating," is even more interesting:

"1.  The compression ratio may be increased by machining, using
any head gasket, or doing without one.

"2.  Reboring the cylinders is authorized on condition that the
greatest bore measurement specified for that make and model is not
exceeded by more than 1.2mm (0.472 inches).

"3. Substitute pistons of any material or origin are authorized.

"4.  The substitution or addition of any valve springs [...]

"5.  Use of any pushrods.

"6.  Any camshaft(s) may be used. [. . .]

"7.  Alternate connecting rods are allowed, providing they are
the same material as original rods and original geometry crank pin
to wrist pin dimension is maintained.

"8.  Alternate rocker arms and attendant assemblies are allowed.
Material(s) is unrestricted.

"9.  Crankshaft main bearing caps mey be substituted and 
additional main mearing caps may be used, provided no material
is added to the block for their attachment. [.. .]"

Get the point?  Unless the Super 90 is the only way to get a 1600,
it's a waste of money (and an interesting street motor) to use it.
The engine you end up with for E Prod will only bear a superficial,
external resemblance to a Porsche motor anyway.  You will want to
run compression up near 13:1, a cam with about 1.5 times the lift
of stock (and yes, that means cutting little notches in the tops
of the pistons so that the valves don't bang into them) and duration
of probably greater than 300 degrees, ports big enough to 
drive your FV through, and rocker assemblies of purest virgin
unobtainium.  Do you already know about carbon-fiber pushrods?
Aluminum flywheels?  Radiusing the crank journals?  Do you
know which connecting rods work the best (I'll bet HCP does)?

And, since we're going to be in the same class when you get this
car finished, do you have any idea why I'm telling you how to
make it go faster? :-)

--Scott "Team Fizzball: From Abingdon to Zuffenhausen" Fisher

Well, yes 'n' no. The Super-90 motor takes a different case, different

	Manufacturer: Porsche
	Model: 356, 356A, 356C-(1500, 1600), 356C/1600SC, 356B Super 90
	       Cabriolet

Now this is interesting. This list is not by any means complete, unless the
punctuation is really screwy. I'll put it vertically to show what I mean:
	
356,
356A,
356C-(1500, 1600),
356C/1600SC,
356B Super 90
Cabriolet

Now, nowhere on this list does it say 356B Normal, nor does it say "Karmann 
Hardtop, Speedster, Roadster, 1600 Super, or any other of the different
types of 356 available......It would seem to indicate that any 356 or 356A
is eligible, and the completely never-did-exist 356C-1500 is eligible, as
are the really-did-exist 356C and 356SC models. It would also seem to 
indicate that any Cabriolet is eligible.  The point is, a 356B Normal Coupe
does not appear under any of those designations. 

I think the spirit, however, is that any 356 is eligible. (with the exception
of the 1300 356, which is eligible for G Prod.) 

	ENGINE
	  Manufacturer         Porsche
	  Type                 OHV, 4 cyl. opposed

Yes, ain't it the truth.  The '64 C coupe has been at home just three
weeks now, and jealousy is rampant in the stable!  The Jetta's battery
open-circuited; the Squareback had a distributor rotor disintegrate and
has taken to rough idling; the Bus has picked up a nasty sticking hydraulic
lifter.  And to make matters worse, the C has responded by crapping all
over the garage floor...  I can see that it will take all winter to
massage bruised egos and make the C feel young again.

Cc: porschephiles <porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu>
Subject: Re: Are European Porsche's
Message-Id: <04901031162240/0003844665NB3EM@mcimail.com>

If you're looking at pre-Carrera (1984) 911s in Europe, some things are very
different.  The compression may be different due to different pistons/heads, the
cam will be different, and the engine management (injection, advance/retard)
will also be different.

And there are a few lesser items: no cat, no fuel pipe restrictor, wrong
bumpers/shocks (and too-low bumper height), no side marker lamps (easily adapted
using factory holes), wrong headlamps and bezels, no door beams, wrong speedo
faceplate, and no emissions certification.  I think all the window glass and
seatbelts are OK.

It's gotten *very* difficult to bring in non-US cars the past few years.  I
suggest calling the DOT or a compliance shop to get the latest deal.  You'll
need a lot of luck!

                JBS

Patty writes:
> Well, now, I can't let that one go by without defending the good name
> of Opel. I loved my GT and didn't let it go until I had a Porsche to
> replace it. (Even then I was still tempted to keep it.)

As Jeff pointed out, I have a fondness for Opels, also.  They
certainly fit the "less is better" philosophy of sports cars.  Not
even a cheesy motor raise the headlights.  Just a big lever in the
car with a mechanical linkage to the lights.  I love it.

Sports cars are built with one purpose: to drive.  If it isn't
essential to driving the car, it doesn't belong there.  Electric
windows?  Ha!  A crank is easier and damn near maintenance free.
Better yet, snap on side screens.  You should be able to feel the
balance.  I guess that's one thing I never felt in the Miata.  Not
only should the car be agile, you should be able to point it with
your right foot.  You should be able to tell you're loading the
suspension on a certain corner or end, not just be able to tell the
car is pitching and yawing.  The car should be an experience to
drive, not just a fun (or status showing) way to get from point A 
to point B.

> Also, what about Toyota MR-2s? And, er, Ferraris and Lamborghinis? :-)

Never had the pleasure of driving, riding in, or researching an MR2.
Toyota seems to be the "put a candy coating on the idea" type car
company, but I can't say for certain about MR2s.

Ferrari and Lambo are a different class.  They're toys, but a
different type of toy.  I don't know what to say about them.

> I'm sure it's difficult to think of every appropriate company just off
> the top of your head. Andy certainly thought of more than I would have.

Well, yeah.  I knew the list was incomplete.  I could add the Rootes
Group, Mercedes (based on several models), Elva, Lotus, Lancia (sort
of), and probably a few others if I thought real hard.

Sports cars are a religious issue, but I try to attend their church
on some Sundays. :-)

andy
